Published Time: Wed, 22 Apr 2026 01:01:10 GMT

Markdown Content:
# A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions 

## ROBBE VAN DEN EEDE ∗, KU Leuven, Department of Computer Science, Belgium and Vrije Universiteit Brussel, Department of Computer Science, Belgium 

## MARC DENECKER, KU Leuven, Department of Computer Science, Belgium 

Background: Inductive definitions are an important form of knowledge. The logic FO(ID) is an extension of classical first-order logic FO with a language construct to express general non-monotone inductive definitions, which was introduced as the outcome of a study of such definitions. Most existing proof systems for inductive definitions impose syntactic constraints on their definitions, thereby excluding many useful and natural definitions. 

Objectives: We aim to obtain a sequent calculus for FO(ID) that supports general non-monotone definitions. 

Methods: We extend an existing sequent calculus LKID by Brotherston and Simpson, founded on the principle of mathematical induction, to a sequent calculus SC FO(ID) for FO(ID). The main challenge in this extension is the accommodation of non-monotone inductive definitions. To overcome this challenge, we draw inspiration from the stable semantics, which is a commonly used semantics in logic programming that is closely related to the well-founded semantics behind FO(ID). We corroborate SC FO(ID) by establishing several proof-theoretical properties and through demonstration on various examples. 

Results: SC FO(ID) covers the non-monotone definitions of FO(ID). It is sound, both w.r.t. the well-founded and the stable semantics. Therefore, SC FO(ID) is also suitable to prove theorems about (fragments of) logic programs under the well-founded and the stable semantics. Due to Gödel’s first incompleteness theorem, no logic capable of capturing the natural numbers is complete. Thus, like LKID, SC FO(ID) cannot be complete, neither w.r.t. the well-founded nor the stable semantics. However, SC FO(ID) is complete for a propositional fragment of FO(ID) w.r.t. the stable semantics, and for a first-order fragment of FO(ID) w.r.t. a weaker Henkin semantics. These results provide guarantees on the strength of SC FO(ID) within the scope of what is theoretically feasible. Cut-elimination does not hold in general for SC FO(ID) , but it holds for a fragment of FO(ID) consisting of positive definitions. This means that theorems in the given fragment can be proven without lemmas. Nevertheless, we prove a weakened version of cut-elimination for a fragment of FO(ID) consisting of stratified definitions. Furthermore, we establish a correspondence between SC FO(ID) -proofs and SC FO -proofs from theories in which each definition is replaced by a natural FO induction scheme. We illustrate the versatility of SC FO(ID) by proving various statements about non-monotone definitions. Incidentally, SC FO(ID) allows for proofs of non-totality of definitions, offering a proof-theoretical analysis of paradoxes in ill-formed non-monotone definitions. 

Conclusions: SC FO(ID) is a theoretically substantiated sequent calculus for FO(ID), enabling formal proofs of theorems involving general inductive definitions. 

1 Introduction 1.1 General Inductive Definitions 

Inductive definitions are an important form of knowledge, as they specify a range of useful notions in mathematics and computer science. A prototypical example is the definition of the natural numbers via the following rules: 

• 0 is a natural number. 

• If 𝑛 is a natural number, then so is its successor 𝑠 (𝑛 ).

> ∗

> arXiv:2604.19382v1 [cs.LO] 21 Apr 2026 2•Van den Eede and Denecker

An inductive definition defines a set by specifying how to construct it. The construction process , often called the induction process, is naturally conceived as a process of iterated rule application, starting from the empty set, and terminating when the set is saturated under application of the rules. The natural number definition is a monotone definition, meaning that the addition of objects to the defined set depends only on the presence of other objects, not on their absence. Some definitions are non-monotone. The 

satisfaction relation |= in propositional logic is a binary relation between structures I (seen as sets of propositional symbols) and propositional formulas 𝜑 (written in terms of ∧ and ¬). It is defined by the following rules: 

• I | = 𝑝 if 𝑝 ∈ I .

• I | = 𝜑 ∧ 𝜓 if I | = 𝜑 and I | = 𝜓 .

• I | = ¬𝜑 if not I | = 𝜑 .The third rule is non-monotone, since it derives pairs (I , ¬𝜑 ) for the defined relation from the absence of pairs 

(I , 𝜑 ) in it. Non-monotone rules complicate the process of iterated rule application. For example, every instance of the third rule is trivially applicable at the start of the construction process, when the set under construction is still empty and hence, “not I | = 𝜑 ” is trivially satisfied. Applying the rule at this point would be unsafe , however, as the construction process may later derive I | = 𝜑 . As argued in [6, 18], application of a non-monotone rule is to be delayed until the truth of its condition is safely established and cannot be falsified anymore. The role of the well-founded induction order associated to this type of definition (here the subformula order on formulas 𝜑 ) is to ensure safety; applying rules along this order, i.e., deriving smaller elements before larger ones, ensures that each rule application is safe. FO(ID) is an extension of classical first-order logic with a language construct to represent a wide range of inductive definitions [ 16 ]. More concretely, definitions are represented in FO(ID) as sets of definitional rules ,which are expressions of the form ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 . Here 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is an atom called the head of the rule, and 𝜑 afirst-order formula called the body of the rule. For instance, the natural number definition from above can be formally represented in FO(ID) as 

 Nat (zero ) ← ⊤ ∀𝑛 : Nat (succ (𝑛 )) ← Nat (𝑛 )



.

The logic FO(ID) has a somewhat convoluted history, with roots outside mathematical logic. Its origins lie in the semantic troubles surrounding the logic programming language Prolog in the 1970s. Prolog programs consist of general program clauses . These are expressions of the form Head :- Body , in which Head consists of an atom, and Body of any (finite) number of literals. For some time, the logic programming community struggled to provide a coherent logical declarative interpretation for Prolog programs. Denecker et al. proposed interpreting Prolog programs as non-monotone inductive definitions [ 14 ]. Denecker and Vennekens showed that the well-founded semantics , a formal semantics developed for logic programs with negation in the body [39], correctly computes the defined relation of non-monotone inductive definitions via three-valued evaluation, without requiring an induction order to ensure the safe application of rules [ 12 , 18 ]. Later, Denecker and Ternovska proposed extending classical first-order logic with a language construct to express non-monotone inductive definitions [ 13 , 16 ]. This resulted in the logic FO(ID), which serves as an expressive knowledge representation language 1 and a formal scientific study of general inductive definitions. Other logics of inductive definitions impose syntactic constraints on their definitions such as positivity 

[ 1 , 32 , 33 , 35 ] and stratification [ 10 , 19 , 26 , 28 ]. Positivity forbids negation in bodies of definitional rules, thus only admitting monotone definitions. Stratification does permit negation in rule bodies, but only such that the   

> 1In the field of Knowledge Representation, first-order logic is commonly used as a modeling language. However, first-order logic has a rather limited expressivity, as it cannot represent certain elementary concepts such the set of natural numbers and the transitive closure of a graph. Adding inductive definitions to first-order logic helps overcome these representational weaknesses. A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions •3

definition can be decomposed into a hierarchy of positive definitions, each depending positively or negatively on concepts defined strictly lower in the hierarchy. Although less restrictive than positivity, stratification still excludes many natural and useful definitions. By not imposing these constraints, FO(ID) offers a general account of inductive definitions. In particular, FO(ID) covers monotone definitions, definitions over a well-founded order, and iterated inductive definitions [10, 19, 26, 28]. By discarding the stratification restriction, FO(ID) admits non-total definitions, for which the construction process terminates in a state where at least one rule is applicable, but no rule is safely applicable. A simple example is {𝑃 ← ¬ 𝑃 }, which defines the propositional symbol 𝑃 as its own negation. In the initial state, where 𝑃 

is false, the single rule of this definition is applicable but not safely, since its application falsifies the condition of the rule. The well-founded model assigns undefined to all atoms that are derivable but not safely derivable. Non-total definitions are related to self-referential paradoxes. For instance, {𝑃 ← ¬ 𝑃 } can be seen as a formalization of the liar paradox : “this sentence is false”, where 𝑃 denotes the truth value of the sentence. Alternatively, consider a version of the barber paradox in which a man named Jim is defined to shave himself, and a barber named Bob is defined to shave all men who do not shave themselves. This can be formalized with the following definition in FO(ID): 

 Shaves (Jim , Jim ) ← ⊤ ∀𝑚 : Shaves (Bob , 𝑚 ) ← ¬ Shaves (𝑚, 𝑚 )



This definition is non-total, as it contains the paradoxical instance Shaves (Bob , Bob ) ← ¬ Shaves (Bob , Bob ).Paradoxical “definitions” are anomalies, to be avoided in practice. But they are interesting anomalies, especially from the perspective of FO(ID) as formal scientific study of inductive definitions. Paradoxical definitions can be stated in natural language, and the logic FO(ID) allows us to study such definitions, and to distinguish them from sensible, i.e., total definitions. Nevertheless, when desired, one can always impose stratification on definitions in FO(ID) to rule out non-total definitions, or other more general conditions as investigated in [16]. 

1.2 A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions 

Deductive inference is an important and useful form of reasoning that occupies a central position in logic. In this paper, we investigate deductive inference for general inductive definitions by introducing a sequent calculus SC FO(ID) for the logic FO(ID). A sequent calculus is a style of proof system due to Gentzen [ 21 ], which is well-known for its theoretical elegance and its goal-directed approach to theorem proving. A proof system for FO(ID) enables formally proving theorems about general inductive definitions. Proof systems have applications in 

formal verification , in which crucial properties of systems are logically derived from their implementation using proof assistants. A more recent application of proof systems is in proof logging [3 , 5 , 23 ], in which combinatorial search algorithms are made certifying [29 ] by generating, alongside the output to a problem instance, a proof of its correctness. This proof is then independently verified by a proof checker. Our sequent calculus SC FO(ID) is based on the principle of mathematical induction , which is a technique to prove theorems about inductively defined sets using induction hypotheses. SC FO(ID) extends the sequent calculus LKID by Brotherston and Simpson [ 9], which formalizes the principle of mathematical induction for positive (hence, monotone) definitions. The main challenge in this extension lies in handling the non-monotonicity of definitions in FO(ID). Our solution is surprisingly simple: in the induction rule, we replace only the positive occurrences of defined predicates by an induction hypothesis. This solution has a theoretical basis in the stable (model) semantics 

from logic programming [ 20 ]. While the stable semantics is no longer about inductive definitions, it can be seen as a good approximation of the well-founded semantics, which coincides with the well-founded semantics on every total (hence, on every ‘sensible’) definition. 4 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

1.3 Related Work 

Multiple proof systems have been developed for inductive definitions. Martin-Löf introduced an intuitionistic natural deduction system (another style of proof system due to Gentzen [ 21 ]) for iterated inductive definitions [ 28 ]. McDowell and Miller [ 30 ] as well as Momigliano and Tiu [ 31 ] provided sequent calculus counterparts to this system. Brotherston and Simpson developed classical sequent calculi for a more restricted class of inductive definitions, formalizing the principles of mathematical induction and infinite descent [ 9 ]. Finally, Hou Ping et al. devised a classical sequent calculus for the propositional fragment of FO(ID) [ 25 ], and Hou Ping and Denecker devised a sequent calculus for an extension of FO(ID) with least fixpoint constructions [24]. The sequent calculi by Brotherston and Simpson are restricted to positive definitions, and the proof systems by Martin-Löf, McDowell and Miller, and Momigliano and Tiu to stratified definitions. The sequent calculus by Hou Ping et al. [ 25 ] supports non-stratified definitions, but only propositional ones. The sequent calculus by Hou Ping and Denecker [ 24 ], however, supports first-order non-stratified definitions. Our sequent calculus SC FO(ID) differs from their calculus, which we here call HD, in the following ways: 

• HD is based more directly on the well-founded semantics, as it uses the notion of unfounded set [ 39 ]. SC FO(ID) , on the other hand, is based on the principle of mathematical induction, extending the sequent calculus LKID by Brotherston and Simpson [9]. 

• HD supports an extension of FO(ID) with least fixpoint expressions [11 ]. Adding least fixpoint expressions to the language of FO(ID) enlarges the class of expressible sets. However, one inference rule for these least fixpoint expressions is infinitary , meaning that it has infinitely many premises. SC FO(ID) only has finitary inference rules, but it does not support least fixpoint expressions. 

• Contrary to HD, SC FO(ID) is sound w.r.t. both the well-founded and the stable semantics. Therefore, SC FO(ID) 

can additionally be used to prove theorems about (fragments of) logic programs under either semantics. 2

The fact that SC FO(ID) is sound w.r.t. the two semantics demarcates its deductive strength, as it can only prove statements that are valid under both semantics. When used to reason about inductive definitions, this constraint is rather minor, since the two semantics correspond on all total definitions. 

1.4 Contributions 

In previous work, we introduced the sequent calculus SC FO(ID) and proved its soundness w.r.t. the well-founded semantics [37]. This paper extends our previous work by adding: 

• a more detailed discussion on the intuitions behind SC FO(ID) and additional examples; 

• an explicit link between SC FO(ID) and the stable semantics; 

• several proof-theoretical results: 

– a correspondence between theorems in SC FO(ID) and theorems in a sequent calculus SC FO for classical first-order logic FO, in which definitions are replaced by an (infinite) approximating FO-schema; 

– soundness w.r.t. the stable semantics; 

– completeness w.r.t. the stable semantics for a propositional fragment of FO(ID) and completeness w.r.t. the weaker Henkin semantics for a first-order fragment of FO(ID); 

– cut-elimination for a fragment of FO(ID) consisting of positive definitions, and a weakened ver-sion of cut-elimination for a fragment consisting of stratified definitions. As shown by multiple counterexamples, cut-elimination does not hold in general for FO(ID).                

> 2SC FO(ID) can not only capture entire logic programs, but also fragments of logic programs, leaving the remainder of the program open. For example, given a logic program that contains facts about an edge relation 𝐸 /2and a definition of the transitive closure 𝑇 /2of 𝐸 /2, SC FO(ID)
> can capture the definition of 𝑇 /2independently of the facts about 𝐸 /2.A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions •5

1.5 Overview 

The paper is structured as follows. In Section 2, we introduce the syntax and the well-founded semantics for FO(ID). In Section 3, we present the sequent calculus SC FO(ID) and prove a few propositions about the inference rules for definitions. In Section 4, we establish a correspondence between theorems of SC FO(ID) and theorems of SC FO . In Section 5, we define the stable semantics and the Henkin semantics for FO(ID). In Sections 6, 7 and 8, we prove the aforementioned theoretical results on soundness, completeness and cut-elimination, respectively. Finally, we provide a summary and possible directions for future research in Section 9. Technical proofs of several lemmas and propositions are included in the appendix. 

2 FO(ID) 

Large parts of this section appear in earlier papers [14, 16, 18]. 

2.1 Syntax 

The syntax of FO(ID) extends the syntax of first-order logic (FO), which is defined as usual. A vocabulary is a set of non-logical symbols, which are subdivided into predicate symbols and function symbols. We write ar (𝜎 )

to refer to the arity of a predicate or function symbol 𝜎 , and 𝜎 /𝑛 to indicate that 𝜎 has arity 𝑛 . A propositional symbol is a 0-ary predicate symbol, and a object symbol is a 0-ary function symbol. Terms are built from function symbols. Atoms are expressions of the form 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ), 𝑡 = 𝑠 , ⊤ or ⊥, where 𝑃 /𝑛 is a predicate symbol, ¯𝑡 an 𝑛 -tuple of terms, 𝑡 and 𝑠 terms, and ⊤ and ⊥ Boolean constants representing truth and falsity, respectively. (FO-) formulas 

are built from atoms with propositional connectives ¬, ∧, ∨, ⇒, ⇔ and quantifiers ∀, ∃. If all non-logical symbols in a formula 𝜑 occur in a vocabulary Σ, we call 𝜑 a formula over Σ. We write 𝑡 ≠ 𝑠 for ¬( 𝑡 = 𝑠 ). We write ¯𝑡 for a tuple of terms (𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑛 ). Given another tuple of terms ¯𝑠 = (𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑠 𝑛 ), we write ¯𝑡 = ¯𝑠 for 𝑡 1 = 𝑠 1 ∧ · · · ∧ 𝑡 𝑛 = 𝑠 𝑛 .Given a tuple of object symbols ¯𝑥 = (𝑥 1, . . . , 𝑥 𝑛 ), we write ∀¯𝑥 : 𝜑 for ∀𝑥 1 : ∀𝑥 2 : . . . ∀𝑥 𝑛 : 𝜑 and similarly for 

∃ ¯𝑥 : 𝜑 . Given terms 𝑡 , 𝑠 and an object symbol 𝑠 , we denote by 𝑡 [𝑠 /𝑥 ] the term obtained from 𝑡 by replacing all occurrences of 𝑥 in 𝑡 by 𝑠 . An occurrence of a object symbol 𝑥 in a formula 𝜑 is said to be bound if it is in the scope of a quantified subformula ∃𝑥 : 𝜓 or ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 of 𝜑 , and is said to be free otherwise. Note that we do not explicitly distinguish between variables and constants. Such distinction can be made implicitly by seeing free occurrences of objects symbols in a formula as constants and bound occurrences as variables. The set of freely occurring object symbols in a formula 𝜑 is denoted by free (𝜑 ). For a set of formulas Γ, we write free (Γ) for 

∪𝜑 ∈Γ free (𝜑 ). Given a term 𝑡 , an object symbol 𝑥 and a formula 𝜑 , we denote by 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] the formula obtained from 𝜑 by replacing all free occurrences of 𝑥 in 𝜑 by 𝑡 . The substitution is safe if 𝑡 contains no object symbol 𝑦 

that gets bound in 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] due to the existence of a free occurrence of 𝑥 in the scope of a quantification ∀𝑦 or ∃𝑦 

within 𝜑 . We will silently assume that such quantifications in 𝜑 are renamed prior to the substitution, so that substitution is always safe. Given a set of formulas Γ, we write Γ[𝑡 /𝑥 ] for the set ∪𝜑 ∈Γ𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ]. An occurrence of a subformula 𝜓 in a formula 𝜑 is said to be positive if it occurs in the scope of an even number of negation symbols ¬ (after unwinding all biconditionals ⇔ and material implications ⇒ in terms of ∧, ∨ and ¬), and is said to be negative otherwise. We use the symbol  for syntactic equality. 

Definition 2.1. A definitional rule (or rule for short) is an expression of the form 

∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 

where ¯𝑥 is a tuple of object symbols, 𝑃 a predicate symbol, ¯𝑡 an ar (𝑃 )-tuple of terms, and 𝜑 an FO-formula. The atom 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is called the head and the formula 𝜑 the body of the definitional rule. An object symbol 𝑦 is said to 

occur freely in a definitional rule ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 if it occurs freely in the head 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) or the body 𝜑 of the rule, and is not among ¯𝑥 . An (FO(ID)-) definition is a finite set Φ of definitional rules such that for any definitional rule 

∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 in Φ, no object symbol in ¯𝑥 occurs freely in any (other) definitional rule in Φ. A predicate symbol 6 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

𝑃 that occurs in the head of a definitional rule of a definition Φ is said to be a defined predicate of Φ. A defined atom is an atom 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) where 𝑃 is a defined predicate of Φ. All other non-logical symbols occurring in Φ are called 

parameters of Φ. The set of defined predicates of Φ is denoted by def (Φ), the set of parameters of Φ by pars (Φ),and their union by sym (Φ). We say that Φ defines its defined predicates in terms of its parameters. An object symbol 𝑦 is said to occur freely in Φ if it occurs freely in a definitional rule of Φ. We denote the set of all freely occurring object symbols in Φ by free (Φ).Inductive definitions define a concept by specifying how to construct its values from the values of its parameter concepts. Intuitively, the construction proceeds by iteratively applying the definitional rules. 3 Concretely, an instance of a definitional rule ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 becomes applicable during the construction process when its body is derived to (safely) hold in the relation being constructed. Applying the rule amounts to extending the value of 𝑃 with the instance of 𝑡 . The connective ← is referred to as the definitional implication . We will see that the presence of a definitional rule in a definition logically entails the corresponding material implication. Yet, the two connectives ← and ⇒ should not be confused. An important difference is that definitional rules are not 

truth-conditional , meaning that they are not assigned truth values in structures. Definitions, on the other hand, are 

truth-conditional in FO(ID), as will be clarified in Section 2.2. Informally, a definition Φ is satisfied in a structure 

I if I interprets the defined predicates of Φ as specified by the rules of Φ.FO(ID) -formulas are built from atoms and definitions with propositional connectives and quantifiers. Hence, definitions are an explicit part of the syntax of FO(ID), and they can occur in complex formulas. 

Example 2.2. Let there be a universe of nodes, let 𝐺 be a graph on them and let 𝑠 be a particular node. A node 𝑡 

is said to be reachable (from 𝑠 , through 𝐺 ) if there exists a path from 𝑠 to 𝑡 along the edges of 𝐺 . We can define the set of reachable nodes from 𝑠 with an FO(ID)-definition of a predicate Reachable /1, in terms of a predicate 

Edge /2, representing the edges of 𝐺 , and a constant 𝑠 :

 Reachable (𝑠 ) ← ⊤ ∀𝑥, 𝑦 : Reachable (𝑦 ) ← Reachable (𝑥 ) ∧ Edge (𝑥, 𝑦 )



Denote this definition by Φ. The FO(ID)-formula ∃𝑠 : Φ ∧ ∀ 𝑡 : Reachable (𝑡 ) expresses that 𝐺 has a node with a path to every other node in 𝐺 , and ∀𝑠 : Φ ∧ ∀ 𝑡 : Reachable (𝑡 ) expresses that every node in 𝐺 has a path to every other node in 𝐺 , i.e., that 𝐺 is strongly connected .Below is an example of a definition by simultaneous or mutual induction .

Example 2.3. The sets of even and odd numbers can be mutually defined by an FO(ID)-definition of a predicate 

Even /2 and a predicate Odd /2, in terms of the successor function succ /1 and the constant zero :



Even (zero ) ← ⊤ ∀𝑛 : Even (succ (𝑛 )) ← Odd (𝑛 )∀𝑛 : Odd (succ (𝑛 )) ← Even (𝑛 )



Several logics [ 10 , 19 , 26 , 28 , 30 , 31 ] support stratified inductive definitions, which are more general than monotone definitions. 

Definition 2.4. Let Φ be a definition. A stratification of Φ is a function ℓ : def (Φ) → N such that for every definitional rule ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 of Φ:(i) ℓ (𝑄 ) ≤ ℓ (𝑃 ) for every 𝑄 ∈ def (Φ) that occurs in 𝜑 ;(ii) ℓ (𝑄 ) < ℓ (𝑃 ) for every 𝑄 ∈ def (Φ) that occurs negatively in 𝜑 .If a stratification of Φ exists, we say that Φ is stratified .   

> 3Martin-Löf refers to definitional rules as productions , emphasizing the constructive nature of inductive definitions. A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions •7

A stratification ℓ of a definition Φ induces a hierarchy of monotone subdefinitions. A stratified definition can be seen as one that is splittable in a hierarchy of monotone subdefinitions. 

Example 2.5. The following definition is stratified: 



Reachable (𝑠 ) ← ⊤ ∀𝑥, 𝑦 : Reachable (𝑦 ) ← Reachable (𝑥 ) ∧ Edge (𝑥, 𝑦 )∀𝑥 : Unreachable (𝑥 ) ← ¬ Reachable (𝑥 )



Indeed, ℓ (Reachable ) = 0 and ℓ (Unreachable ) = 1 specify a stratification for this definition. A stratified definition can be seen as one that can be split up in a hierarchy of monotone subdefinitions. For the definition in the previous example, for instance, this hierarchy consists of the monotone subdefinitions 

 Reachable (𝑠 ) ← ⊤ ∀𝑥, 𝑦 : Reachable (𝑦 ) ← Reachable (𝑥 ) ∧ Edge (𝑥, 𝑦 )



and  ∀𝑥 : Unreachable (𝑥 ) ← ¬ Reachable (𝑥 ) .

Even though stratified definitions are more general than monotone definitions, there are many examples of inductive definitions that are not stratified: 

Example 2.6. While commonly defined via a monotone definition, the set of even natural numbers can also be defined via a non-monotone definition by structural induction. In FO(ID), it would be expressed as follows: 

 Even (zero ) ← ⊤ ∀𝑛 : Even (succ (𝑛 )) ← Nat (𝑛 ) ∧ ¬ Even (𝑛 )



This definition defines the predicate Even /1 in terms of the constant zero , the function succ /1 and the natural number predicate Nat /1. There exists no stratification ℓ of this definition, as the second definitional rule would impose ℓ (Even ) < ℓ (Even ).

Example 2.7. The satisfaction relation |= for propositional logic can be defined by an FO(ID)-definition of a predicate Sat /2, in terms of a predicate Member /2 and constructor functions and /2 and not /1:



∀𝑖, 𝑝 : Sat (𝑖, 𝑝 ) ← Member (𝑝, 𝑖 )∀𝑖, 𝑓 , 𝑔 : Sat (𝑖, 𝑎𝑛𝑑 (𝑓 , 𝑔 )) ← Sat (𝑖, 𝑓 ) ∧ Sat (𝑖, 𝑔 )∀𝑖, 𝑓 : Sat (𝑖, 𝑛𝑜𝑡 (𝑓 )) ← ¬ Sat (𝑖, 𝑓 )



The variable 𝑖 represents a structure, 𝑝 a propositional symbol, and 𝑓 and 𝑔 propositional formulas. The term 

𝑎𝑛𝑑 (𝑓 , 𝑔 ) represents the conjunction of 𝑓 and 𝑔 , and 𝑛𝑜𝑡 (𝑓 ) the negation of 𝑓 . The formula Member (𝑝, 𝑖 ) expresses that 𝑝 is an element of 𝑖 , when viewing 𝑖 as a set of propositional symbols. By a similar reasoning as in Example 2.6, this definition is not stratified. 

Example 2.8. The distance from a node 𝑛 to a node 𝑚 in a graph is the length of the shortest path from 𝑛 to 𝑚 

if such path exists, and is undefined otherwise. This notion can be defined by an FO(ID)-definition of a predicate 

Distance /3, containing all triples (𝑥, 𝑦, 𝑛 ) for which 𝑥 and 𝑦 are nodes in the graph with distance 𝑛 from 𝑥 to 𝑦 , in terms of the constant zero , function succ /1, predicate Edge /2, and order relation ≤/ 2 (used with infix notation): 

 ∀𝑥 : Distance (𝑥, 𝑥, zero ) ← ⊤ ∀𝑥, 𝑦, 𝑛 : Distance (𝑥, 𝑦, 𝑠𝑢𝑐𝑐 (𝑛 )) ← (∃ 𝑧 : Distance (𝑥, 𝑧, 𝑛 ) ∧ Edge (𝑧, 𝑦 )) ∧ ¬(∃ 𝑚 : 𝑚 ≤ 𝑛 ∧ Distance (𝑥, 𝑦, 𝑚 )) 



Since the defined predicate Distance occurs negatively in the body of the second rule, this definition is neither monotone nor stratified. 8 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

Example 2.9. Consider a graph in which edges can be activated at each time point. Once activated, an edge remains active until a path of active edges emerges from a given node 𝑎 to a given node 𝑏 . If this happens, all edges become inactive again. The state of the active edges can be modeled by an FO(ID)-definition of a predicate Active /3,together with an auxiliary predicate ActivePath /3, in terms of the function succ /1 and predicate Activate /3:



∀𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 : Active (𝑛, 𝑚, succ (𝑡 )) ← Activate (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ∧ ¬ ActivePath (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑡 )∀𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 : Active (𝑛, 𝑚, succ (𝑡 )) ← Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ∧ ¬ ActivePath (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑡 )∀𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 : ActivePath (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ← Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 )∀𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑝, 𝑡 : ActivePath (𝑛, 𝑝, 𝑡 ) ← ActivePath (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ∧ ActivePath (𝑚, 𝑝, 𝑡 )



The atom Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) says that the edge from 𝑛 to 𝑚 is active at time point 𝑡 , Activate (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) says that this edge is activated at time point 𝑡 , and ActivePath (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) says that there is a path of active edges from 𝑛 to 𝑚 at time point 𝑡 . Time is modeled by the natural numbers. The last two rules define the transitive closure of all active edges at a given time point. This definition does not admit a stratification ℓ, as the third and the first rule would impose ℓ (Active ) ≤ ℓ (ActivePath ) < ℓ (Active ).Through variations, this abstract example can be converted into real-world examples, such as the creation of electric circuits that burn through once a short circuit has been formed, or the construction of towers that collapse after a threshold height/weight has been exceeded. 

Example 2.10. Consider a system with a multiple users and a file. A user has access to the file if they are the owner, or if they are granted access by a user with access while not blocked by a user with access. The delegation of access can be modeled by an FO(ID)-definition of a predicate Access /1 in terms of a constant owner 

and predicates Grants /2 and Blocks /2:

 Access (owner ) ← ⊤ ∀𝑢 : Access (𝑢 ) ← (∃ 𝑣 : Access (𝑣 ) ∧ Grants (𝑣, 𝑢 )) ∧ ¬(∃ 𝑣 : Access (𝑣 ) ∧ Blocks (𝑣, 𝑢 )) 



This definition is not stratified, as the defined predicate Access occurs negatively in the body of the second rule. In general, a definition Φ contains multiple rules ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 per predicate 𝑃 . These rules can be merged into a single rule ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ← 𝜑 𝑃, Φ ( ¯𝑦 ) without altering the (informal) meaning of the definition. 

Definition 2.11. A definition Φ is said to be normal if, for every defined predicate 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ), there is precisely one definitional rule in Φ with 𝑃 in its head, which is of the form ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ← 𝜑 . The normalization ΦN of a definition Φ is the definition consisting of one rule ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ← 𝜑 𝑃, Φ ( ¯𝑦 ) per defined predicate 𝑃 of Φ, where 

𝜑 𝑃, Φ ( ¯𝑦 ) is the of all formulas ∃ ¯𝑥 : ¯𝑦 = ¯𝑡 ∧ 𝜑 such that ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 is a rule in Φ.4 Here ¯𝑦 does not include object symbols quantified in front of a rule in Φ defining 𝑃 . We refer to 𝜑 𝑃, Φ ( ¯𝑦 ) as the merged body of 𝑃 in Φ w.r.t. ¯𝑦 . We will often write 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) for 𝜑 𝑃, Φ ( ¯𝑦 ) if Φ is clear from the context. Given an ar (𝑃 )-tuple of terms ¯𝑡 , we write 𝜑 𝑃, Φ (¯𝑡 )

for 𝜑 𝑃, Φ ( ¯𝑦 ) [ ¯𝑡 / ¯𝑦 ], which we call the merged body of 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ).

2.2 Well-founded Semantics 

For monotone inductive definitions, the standard construction process starts from the assumption that all defined facts are false, and then gradually revises some of these assumptions through iterated rule application. Once saturation is reached, i.e., once there are no more applicable rules, it concludes that all defined atoms that have not been derived to be true must be false. At each point of the construction process, partial information about the defined atoms is available: atoms that are true at an intermediate stage have reached their defined value, but atoms that are false at an intermediate stage not necessarily; these may still be derived true at a later stage. The construction process for monotone definitions does not work for non-monotone definitions, since non-monotone rules can only be applied safely when it is certain that the negative subformulas ¬𝜓 in their bodies are true, i.e., that 𝜓 will not be derived later in the construction process. Based on the well-founded semantics for logic programs [ 39 ], Denecker and Vennekens introduced a new formalization of the construction process [ 18 ]. By re-formalizing the construction process as a sequence of three-valued structures of increasing precision, it was made explicit at each stage of the process whether a defined fact was derived to be true ( t), derived to be false ( f), or not yet derived, i.e., still unknown ( u). As in the monotone case, a fact is derived to be true if the body of one of its rules is true in the current three-valued structure. Deriving an unknown fact to be (certainly) false is more subtle. It uses the notion of unfounded set [ 39 ], which is a set of unknown atoms such that, if all of these atoms are set to false, then the bodies of all the rules deriving these facts become false as well. Intuitively, the atoms in an unfounded set can only make each other true, and therefore, they can be safely derived to be false. We we will formalize the aforementioned notions in the remainder of this section. (Two-valued) structures I are defined as usual, consisting of a set 𝐷 called the domain of I, also denoted by 

dom (Φ), and a mapping from non-logical symbols 𝜎 to appropriate values 𝜎 I in 𝐷 , called the interpretation of 𝜎 

in I. In particular, 𝜎 I ⊆ 𝐷 𝑛 if 𝜎 is an 𝑛 -ary predicate symbol, and 𝜎 I : 𝐷 𝑛 → 𝐷 if 𝜎 is an 𝑛 -ary function symbol. By abuse of notation, we will view the interpretation of a propositional symbol as a Boolean constant, f or t

(standing for ‘false’ and ‘true’, respectively), and the interpretation of an object symbol as an element of 𝐷 . The set of interpreted symbols of I is called the vocabulary voc (I) of I. If voc (I) = Σ, we cal I a Σ-structure . We say that a structure I interprets an expression 𝜖 (a term or a formula) if all freely occurring non-logical symbols in 𝜖 are interpreted in I. In this case, we call 𝜖 an expression over voc (I) . Given a tuple of terms ¯𝑡 = (𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑛 )

interpreted by a structure I, we write ¯𝑡 I for (𝑡 I 

> 1

, . . . , 𝑡 I 

> 𝑛

).We say that a structure I expands a structure J if voc (J ) ⊆ voc (I) , dom (I) = dom (J ) and 𝜎 I = 𝜎 J for all 

𝜎 ∈ voc (J ) . Given a structure I with domain 𝐷 , a tuple of non-logical symbols ¯𝜎 , and a tuple of corresponding values ¯𝛼 in 𝐷 , we denote by I [ ¯𝜎 : ¯𝛼 ] the structure obtained from I by interpreting ¯𝜎 by ¯𝛼 . If Σ is a subset of the vocabulary of I, then I| Σ denotes the structure obtained from I by restricting its interpretation mapping to Σ.Denecker and Vennekens formalize the construction processes behind inductive definitions as well-founded inductions , which are (possibly transfinite) sequences of increasingly precise three-valued structures [ 18 ]. These structures have three different truth values : f, u and t, where u stands for ‘unknown’. We consider two orders on the set of truth values: the truth order ≤t and the precision order ≤p, defined by f ≤t u ≤t t, and u ≤p f, u ≤p t

respectively. We also write ¬f ≔ t, ¬u ≔ u and ¬t ≔ f. A three-valued structure 𝔄 is defined similarly as a two-valued structure, except that 𝑛 -ary predicate symbols are interpreted as functions from 𝐷 𝑛 to {f, u, t}, where 

𝐷 is the domain of 𝔄 . We extend the truth and precision order pointwisely to functions with codomain {f, u, t},i.e., given 𝑓 , 𝑔 : 𝑋 → { f, u, t}, we let 𝑓 ≤t 𝑔 iff 𝑓 (𝑥 ) ≤ t 𝑔 (𝑥 ) for all 𝑥 ∈ 𝑋 , and similarly for ≤p. Furthermore, we extend these orders to three-valued structures 𝔄 , 𝔅 , by letting 𝔄 ≤t 𝔅 iff 𝔄 and 𝔅 have the same domain and vocabulary, the same interpretation for non-predicate symbols, and 𝑃 𝔄 ≤t 𝑃 𝔅 for every predicate symbol 𝑃 , and similarly for ≤p. Note that a two-valued structure can be seen as a special case of a three-valued structure, by identifying a subset 𝑆 of 𝐷 𝑛 with its characteristic function, sending every element of 𝑆 to t and every non-element to f. In this way, two-valued structures correspond to maximally precise three-valued structures. In the sequel, 10 • Van den Eede and Denecker

Definition 2.14. A well-founded induction of Φ in O is a sequence ⟨𝔄 𝑖 ⟩0≤𝑖 ≤𝛽 of three-valued sym (Φ)-structures extending O, such that: 

• 𝑃 𝔄 0 ( ¯𝑎 ) = u for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ) and all ¯𝑎 ∈ dom (𝔄 0)ar (𝑃 ) ;

• 𝔄 𝑖 +1 is a Φ-refinement of 𝔄 𝑖 for every ordinal 0 ≤ 𝑖 < 𝛽 ; and 

• 𝔄 𝜆 is the ≤p-limit of ⟨𝔄 𝑖 ⟩𝑖 <𝜆 for every limit ordinal 0 < 𝜆 ≤ 𝛽 .A well-founded induction ⟨𝔄 𝑖 ⟩0≤𝑖 ≤𝛽 is called terminal if its limit 𝔄 𝛽 has no Φ-refinement. The well-founded model 

of Φ in O is the limit of any terminal well-founded induction of Φ in O. If this well-founded model is two-valued, we say that Φ is total in O. If Φ is total in any Φ-context, we say that Φ is total .

Remark 2.15 . Note that well-founded inductions are ≤p-increasing, which guarantees that the ≤p-limits are well-defined. Since the truth function is ≤p-monotone, well-founded inductions ⟨𝔄 𝑖 ⟩0≤𝑖 ≤𝛽 have the property that once 𝜑 𝔄 𝑖 ∈ { f, t} for some 𝑖 , then 𝜑 𝔄 𝑖 = 𝜑 𝔄 𝑗 for all 𝑗 ≥ 𝑖 .The following result by Denecker and Vennekens [ 17 ] guarantees that the notion of well-founded model of a definition in a context is well-defined. 

Theorem 2.16. Any definition has a terminal well-founded induction in any context. Furthermore, all terminal well-founded inductions of a given definition in a given context have the same limit. 

Non-totality of Φ in O means that the well-founded induction of Φ in O leave at least one defined atom unknown. This usually indicates a kind of flaw in the definition (or in the interpretation of the parameters). 

Example 2.17. Let Φ be the even number definition from Example 2.6, and let O be the Φ-context with

...𝐸 𝔄 𝜔 (𝑛 ) = t for 𝑛 ∈ 2N, 𝐸 𝔄 𝜔 (𝑛 ) = f for 𝑛 ∈ 2N + 1

specify a terminal well-founded induction ⟨𝔄 𝑖 ⟩0≤𝑖 ≤𝜔 of Φ in O (in fact, the only one). Here 𝜔 denotes the first infinite ordinal. Thus, the well-founded model of Φ in O′ is three-valued, and therefore, Φ is non-total in O′.The well-founded satisfaction relation |=wf is a binary relation between two-valued structures I and FO(ID)-formulas 𝜑 of which all non-logical symbols are interpreted by I, defined by the following rules: 

• I | =wf 𝑡 = 𝑠 if 𝑡 I = 𝑠 I ;

• I | =wf 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) if ¯𝑡 ∈ 𝑃 I ;12 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

• I | =wf ¬𝜑 if not I | =wf 𝜑 ;

• I | =wf 𝜑 ∧ 𝜓 if I | =wf 𝜑 and I | =wf 𝜓 ;

• I | =wf ∀𝑥 : 𝜑 if I [ 𝑥 : 𝑎 ] | =wf 𝜑 for all 𝑎 ∈ dom (I) ;

• I | =wf Φ if I| sym (Φ) is the well-founded model of Φ in I| pars (Φ) .The rules for ∨, ⇒, ⇔ and ∃ can de derived from the above rules through reformulation in terms of ¬, ∧ and ∀.An FO(ID)-formula 𝜑 is said to be valid or a model (under the well-founded semantics) if I | =wf 𝜑 for all structures 

I interpreting the non-logical symbols of 𝜑 . Two FO(ID)-formulas 𝜑 and 𝜓 are said to be equivalent (under the well-founded semantics) if 𝜑 ⇔ 𝜓 is valid under the well-founded semantics. 

Remark 2.18 . Under the well-founded semantics, every FO(ID)-definition Φ is equivalent to its normalization 

ΦN, i.e., I | =wf Φ iff I | =wf ΦN for all structures I. This is because the well-founded model of a definition in a context is not defined in terms of the bodies of the individual rules, only in terms of the merged bodies 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ).A distinctive feature of FO(ID) is that it provides a declarative account of inductive definitions while acknowl-edging their constructive nature. FO(ID) differs from the convention [ 1 , 9 , 10 , 19 , 26 , 28 , 30 – 33 , 35 ] in that it incorporates inductive definitions in its syntax and gives them a truth-conditional treatment. We claim that the truth-conditional account of inductive definitions in FO(ID) is natural, as it corresponds to the truth-conditional account of non-inductive definitions in FO by means of equivalences. For instance, the concept of sibling can be defined in FO by the formula ∀𝑥, 𝑦 : SiblingOf (𝑥, 𝑦 ) ⇔ ∃ 𝑧 : ParentOf (𝑧, 𝑥 ) ∧ ParentOf (𝑧, 𝑦 ). In FO(ID), this defini-tion can equally be represented as the definition {∀ 𝑥, 𝑦 : SiblingOf (𝑥, 𝑦 ) ← ∃ 𝑧 : ParentOf (𝑧, 𝑥 ) ∧ ParentOf (𝑧, 𝑦 )} .In fact, any FO-formula of the form ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑥 ) ⇔ 𝜑 where 𝜑 does not contain 𝑃 is semantically equivalent to the FO(ID)-definition {∀ ¯𝑥 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑥 ) ← 𝜑 }, as can easily be checked. An advantage of the explicit treatment of definitions in FO(ID), is that it allows for the expression of statements about definitions themselves , instead of only about defined predicates. For instance, we can express the equivalence of two inductive definitions Φ1 and Φ2 in FO(ID) as Φ1 ⇔ Φ2. The negation ¬Φ of a definition Φ is sensible as well, as it relates to non-totality. More concretely, if ¬Φ is a valid, then Φ has no two-valued models. Hence, its well-founded model in any context is strictly three-valued. In particular, this implies that Φ is non-total. More generally, given a formula 𝜑 constraining the parameters of Φ, the FO(ID)-formula 𝜑 ⇒ Φ is valid iff the well-founded model of Φ in any Φ-context satisfying 

𝜑 is two-valued. We conclude this section by providing two properties of the well-founded semantics that will lay the basis of the sequent calculus SC FO(ID) for FO(ID). 

Proposition 2.19. Let Φ be a definition, 𝑃 a defined predicate of Φ, and ¯𝑦 an ar (𝑃 )-tuple of object symbols not occurring freely in the body 𝜑 of any definitional rule of Φ of the form ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 . Let I a (two-valued)

For monotone definitions, the defined predicates 𝑃 of Φ can be characterized as the smallest relations that respect the definitional rules of Φ, i.e., that satisfy the material implications ∀¯𝑥 : 𝜑 ⇒ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) corresponding to the definitional rules ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 of Φ. 3 A Sequent Calculus for FO(ID) 

To enable formal proofs for general inductive definitions, we introduce the sequent calculus SC FO(ID) for FO(ID). A sequent calculus SC for a logic L consists of a number of inference rules, formulated in terms of sequents .An ( L-) sequent is an expression of the form Γ ⊢ Δ where Γ and Δ are sets of L-formulas. 8 Unless mentioned otherwise, we assume Γ and Δ to be finite. By convention, we usually discard the curly brackets { } for the sets Γ                

> 7Since the second-order variables 𝐺 Πare not fixed, the induction axiom for Φand Πis strictly speaking ill-defined. However, since the exact choice of variables is semantically irrelevant, we speak of the induction axiom by abuse of terminology.
> 8In other versions, Γand Δare often seen as multisets or lists. 14 •Van den Eede and Denecker

and Δ in a sequent Γ ⊢ Δ. Informally, a sequent Γ ⊢ Δ says that if all formulas in Γ are true, then some formula in 

Δ is true. Hence, a sequent can be seen as a formula Ó Γ ⇒ Ô Δ (if Γ and Δ are finite). Formally, we extend the satisfaction relation |=L of L to sequents Γ ⊢ Δ by letting I | =L Γ ⊢ Δ if I | =L 𝜑 for a formula 𝜑 ∈ Δ or I̸ | =L 𝜑 

for a formula 𝜑 ∈ Γ. An ( SC )-derivation of a sequent Γ ⊢ Δ from sequents Γ1 ⊢ Δ1, . . . , Γ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑛 is a finite rooted labeled directed tree where the nodes are labeled with L-sequents, the root has label Γ ⊢ Δ, the leafs have labels 

Γ1 ⊢ Δ1, . . . , Γ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑛 , and the edges respect the inference rules of SC . By the latter, we mean that for every node 𝑛 

in the tree, there is an instantiation of an inference rule in SC such that the label of 𝑛 is the conclusion and the labels of its children are the premises of this instantiation. An ( SC )-proof of a sequent Γ ⊢ Δ is an SC -derivation of Γ ⊢ Δ from sequents Γ1 ⊢ Δ1, . . . , Γ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑛 that occur as the conclusions of instantiations of inference rules in 

SC without premises. The height of an ( SC -)proof 𝑇 is the largest distance of a path from the root to a leaf in 

𝑇 . We say that a sequent Γ ⊢ Δ is ( SC )-provable or an ( SC )-theorem if there exists an SC -proof of Γ ⊢ Δ. By convention, we write SC -derivations in the typical sequent calculus style with horizontal bars representing applications of inference rules, which we label with the name of the rule: 

Γ1 ⊢ Δ1 . . . Γ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑛 (rule) 

Γ ⊢ Δ

The sequents Γ1 ⊢ Δ1, . . . , Γ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑛 above the bar are the premises of the rule, and the sequent Γ ⊢ Δ below the bar is the conclusion. 

3.1 A Sequent Calculus for FO 

Brotherston and Simpson’s sequent calculus LKID for first-order logic with positive inductive definitions extends Gentzen’s original sequent calculus LK for classical first-order logic [ 9]. Since Gentzen did not consider equality, Brotherston and Simpson complemented LK with two rules for equality. Figure 1 contains the inference rules for FO as presented by Brotherston and Simpson. Some rules are only applicable under certain conditions, which are written next to them. The rule (subst) is only applicable if no quantified object symbol in Γ ∪ Δ occurs in 𝑡 , by which we mean that if Γ or Δ contains a formula of the form ∀𝑦 : 𝜑 or ∃𝑦 : 𝜑 , then 𝑦 does not occur in 𝑡 . We denote the sequent calculus consisting of these inference rules by SC FO . Every FO-connective comes with both a 

left and right introduction rule in SC FO , which introduce the connective in a formula on the left and the right side of the sequent in the conclusion, respectively. We will sometimes apply the rules (subst), ( ∀L), ( ∀R), ( ∃L), ( ∃R), ( =L) and ( =R) directly to tuples of terms ¯𝑡 . The structural rules (wk) and (subst) are admissible in SC FO [ 9 ], meaning that their removal does not affect the set of theorems. In other words, every sequent that is SC FO -provable with (wk) and (subst) is also provable without (wk) and (subst). More substantial is the admissibility of the cut rule (cut) in SC FO . This was originally shown by Gentzen for LK, 9 a result known as his Hauptsatz or cut-elimination theorem [ 21 ]. The cut rule formalizes the introduction of a lemma in a proof. Note that the cut formula 𝜑 in its premises, which represents the lemma, does not occur in the conclusion. This is an obstacle for proof search, as 𝜑 

can be any FO-formula. However, the cut-elimination theorem says this rule can always be avoided. 10 

3.2 Extension to FO(ID) 

We extend SC FO to a sequent calculus SC FO(ID) that accommodates FO(ID)-definitions, by providing a left and a right introduction rule for defined atoms. The right introduction rule for defined atoms is

Here Φ is a definition with a definitional rule ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 , and ¯𝑠 is a tuple of object symbols with the same length as ¯𝑥 . Intuitively, (def R) allows us to derive heads of definitional rules from bodies. The left introduction rule for defined atoms involves the selection of a subset Π of def (Φ) containing a defined predicate 𝑃 of Φ, as well as an FO-formula 𝐹 𝑄 for every 𝑄 ∈ Π, called the induction hypothesis associated with 𝑄 . For every 𝑄 ∈ Π, we also pick an ar (𝑄 )-tuple ¯𝑧 of distinct object symbols that may occur freely in 𝐹 𝑄 .We will often denote an induction hypothesis 𝐹 𝑄 by 𝐹 𝑄 [ ¯𝑧 ] to indicate that ¯𝑧 is the chosen tuple of object symbols for 𝑄 . Given an ar (𝑄 )-tuple of terms ¯𝑠 , we use 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ] as shorthand notation for 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 /¯𝑧 ].minor premises Γ, Φ, 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ Δ (def L) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ

The minor premises consist of all sequents of the form 

Γ, Φ,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ

where ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 is a definitional rule of Φ with 𝑄 ∈ Π. Here 𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] denotes the formula obtained from 

𝜓 by replacing all positive occurrences of atoms 𝑅 ( ¯𝑢 ) in 𝜓 with 𝑅 ∈ Π by 𝐹 𝑅 [ ¯𝑢 ]. This inference rule requires that 16 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

for all definitional rules ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 in Φ, the object symbols in ¯𝑦 do not occur freely in Γ, Δ or Φ. The premise 

Γ, Φ, 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ Δ is called the major premise of the rule. 11 

We also call this inference rule the induction rule , as it is based on the principle of mathematical induction ,in which the elements of inductively defined sets are shown to satisfy a certain property, by finding a suitable induction hypothesis that entails the property, and that is preserved under the definitional rules. The latter is formalized by the minor premises, and the former by the major premise. 

Example 3.1. Let Φ be the natural number definition from Section 1. Suppose we want to prove that a generic natural number 𝑥 satisfies a property expressed by an FO-formula 𝜑 . Then we can do so with the left introduction rule for defined atoms, picking Γ = ∅ and Δ = {𝜑 }:12 

Φ ⊢ 𝐹 Nat [zero ], 𝜑 Φ, 𝐹 Nat (𝑛 ) ⊢ 𝐹 Nat [succ (𝑛 )] , 𝜑 Φ, 𝐹 Nat [𝑥 ] ⊢ 𝜑 (def L) 

Φ, Nat (𝑥 ) ⊢ 𝜑 

Here Π = {Nat }, since Nat is the only defined predicate of Φ. We need to find a suitable induction hypothesis 

𝐹 Nat (𝑥 ). To satisfy the major premise, this induction hypothesis must entail 𝜑 in all models of Φ. To satisfy the minor premises, it must respect the definitional rules of Φ in all models of Φ, in the sense that it must hold for 

zero , and it must hold for succ (𝑛 ) if it holds for 𝑛 (or 𝜑 must hold, but that is precisely what we want to prove). In general, the set Γ allows adding any number of assumptions, and Δ allows replacing 𝜑 by any disjunction of formulas. In particular, we can prove that an object is not a natural number by picking Δ = ∅.Induction hypotheses in (def L) can be any FO-formula. However, only certain choices are suitable to derive the premises of the rule. Finding such induction hypotheses generally requires some creativity. In the minor premises of (def L), only the positive occurrences of defined predicates are replaced by an induction hypothesis. Replacing negative occurrences as well would result in an unsound rule: 

Example 3.2. Let (naive def L) be the alternative to (def L) that does not discriminate between positive and negative occurrences of defined predicates, i.e., the minor premises are of the form Γ, Φ,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ

instead of Γ, Φ,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ. Let Φ𝐸 be the definition of Even from Example 2.6 and let Φ𝑂 be the definition 

{∀ 𝑛 : Odd (succ (𝑛 )) ← Nat (𝑛 ) ∧ ¬ Odd (𝑛 )} .

Using (naive def L), we can derive the sequent Φ𝐸 , Φ𝑂 , Odd (𝑥 ) ⊢ Even (𝑥 ), which is clearly invalid: (ax) 

Φ𝐸 , Φ𝑂 , Nat (𝑛 ) ∧ ¬ Even (𝑛 ) ⊢ Nat (𝑛 ) ∧ ¬ Even (𝑛 ), Even (𝑥 ) (def R) 

Φ𝐸 , Φ𝑂 , Nat (𝑛 ) ∧ ¬ Even (𝑛 ) ⊢ Even (succ (𝑛 )) , Even (𝑥 ) (ax) 

Φ𝐸 , Φ𝑂 , Even (𝑥 ) ⊢ Even (𝑥 ) (naive def L) 

Φ𝐸 , Φ𝑂 , Odd (𝑥 ) ⊢ Even (𝑥 )

Here we applied (naive def L) to Φ𝑂 and Odd (𝑥 ), with 𝐹 Odd [𝑥 ]  Even (𝑥 ) (and Π = {Odd }). Replacing negative occurrences of defined predicates by induction hypotheses generally yields too strong assumptions. Intuitively, this can be understood by viewing an induction hypothesis as describing an upper bound on a defined set: it holds for all element of the set, but possibly also for other objects. Hence, the negation of an induction hypothesis does not necessarily hold for all non-elements of the defined set. 13 

The sequent calculus SC FO(ID) differs from LKID in the following aspects:           

> 11 The terms minor premise and major premise date back to Aristotle’s work on syllogisms [34].
> 12 Strictly speaking, the first premise should be Φ,⊤ ⊢ 𝐹 Nat (zero ), 𝜑 .
> 13 This intuition suggests that, instead of not replacing negative occurrences of defined predicates, we could replace them with formulas describing lower bounds on these predicates. We come back to this idea in the discussion of future work in Section 9. A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions •17

• LKID does not (need to) differentiate between positive and negative occurrences of defined predicates, as Brotherston and Simpson only consider positive definitions. 

• Sequents in LKID do not contain definitions, as definitions are not seen as formulas in the logic of Brotherston and Simpson. Instead, definitions are specified separately in their logic, and LKID-provable sequents are valid only in models of these definitions. 

• In LKID, the set Π in (def L) is fixed as the set of all defined predicates of Φ that are mutually dependent 

with 𝑃 , as will be explained in Section 3.5. The following proposition establishes a deductive equivalence between definitions and their normalizations. 

Proposition 3.3. Let Γ and Δ be sets of FO(ID) -formulas and let Φ be a definition. Let ΦN be the normalization of Φ. Then Γ, Φ ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable iff Γ, ΦN ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. 

Proof. See Appendix B. □

3.3 Alternative Inference Rules 

Judging from (def R), one may have expected the following left introduction rule for defined atoms: 

Γ, Φ, 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ (def L’) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ

While sound, this rule is rather weak. This is because 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) generally contains defined predicates, which need to be introduced by the same rule, often resulting in a circular reasoning: 

Example 3.4. Let Φ be the following definition of a predicate 𝑇 /2 in terms of a predicate 𝐸 /2:

 ∀𝑥, 𝑦 : 𝑇 (𝑥, 𝑦 ) ← 𝐸 (𝑥, 𝑦 )∀𝑥, 𝑦, 𝑧 : 𝑇 (𝑥, 𝑧 ) ← 𝐸 (𝑥, 𝑦 ) ∧ 𝑇 (𝑦, 𝑧 )



It defines 𝑇 as the transitive closure of 𝐸 . Suppose we want to prove a sequent of the form Γ, Φ,𝑇 (𝑎, 𝑏 ) ⊢ Δ with (def L’). Then we generally need to complete the following derivation: 

Γ, Φ, 𝐸 (𝑎, 𝑏 ) ⊢ ΔΓ, Φ, 𝐸 (𝑎, 𝑦 ),𝑇 (𝑦, 𝑏 ) ⊢ Δ (∧R) 

Γ, Φ, 𝐸 (𝑎, 𝑦 ) ∧ 𝑇 (𝑦, 𝑏 ) ⊢ Δ (∃R) 

Γ, Φ, ∃𝑦 : 𝐸 (𝑎, 𝑦 ) ∧ 𝑇 (𝑦, 𝑏 ) ⊢ Δ (∨L) 

Γ, Φ, 𝐸 (𝑎, 𝑏 ) ∨ ∃ 𝑦 : 𝐸 (𝑎, 𝑦 ) ∧ 𝑇 (𝑦, 𝑏 ) ⊢ Δ (def L’) 

Γ, Φ,𝑇 (𝑎, 𝑏 ) ⊢ Δ

Here we assumed that 𝑦 ∉ free (Γ ∪ Δ). Hence, to prove the sequent Γ, Φ,𝑇 (𝑎, 𝑏 ) ⊢ Δ, we would generally need to prove the sequent Γ, Φ, 𝐸 (𝑎, 𝑦 ),𝑇 (𝑦, 𝑏 ) ⊢ Δ, which again has the defined predicate 𝑇 on its left side. 

Proposition 3.5. The inference rule (def L’) is admissible in SC FO(ID) .

Proof. We show that whenever the premise Γ, Φ, 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ of an instantiation of (def R) is SC FO(ID) -provable, then so is the conclusion Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ. Suppose that Γ, Φ, 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. Then, by (wk), so is 

Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ), 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ. (1) Furthermore, the sequent 

Γ, Φ, 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ), Δ (2) is SC FO(ID) -provable. This follows from results of subsequent sections: by Proposition 5.5, the sequent (2) is valid under the Henkin semantics , and by Theorem 6.1, this entails that it is SC FO(ID) -provable. Applying (cut) to (1) and (2), we find that Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. □18 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

Another possible left introduction rule for defined atoms would be the following: minor premises (def L”) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ], Δ

Here the minor premises are as in (def L). Following [ 9 ], we opt for (def L) over (def L”), as (def L) enables cut-elimination (for a fragment of FO(ID) in our case). In fact, (def L) can be seen as a combination of (def L’) and a cut on the induction hypothesis, as illustrated in the proof of Proposition 3.6. In terms of provability (with cut), the rules (def L) and (def L”) are interchangeable in SC FO(ID) , as made precise by the following two propositions: 

Proposition 3.6. The inference rule (def L”) is admissible in SC FO(ID) .

Proof. We show that whenever the premises of an instantiation of (def L”) are SC FO(ID) -provable, then so is the conclusion Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ], Δ. Suppose that the premises are SC FO(ID) -provable. These are of the form 

Γ, Φ,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ, where ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 is a definitional rule of Φ with 𝑄 ∈ Π. By (wk), the sequents 

Γ, Φ,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ], Δ (3) are SC FO(ID) -provable for every definitional rule ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 of Φ with 𝑄 ∈ Π. By (ax), so is 

Γ, Φ, 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ], Δ. (4) Applying (def L) to (3) and (4), we find that Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ]Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. □

Proposition 3.7. Let SC (def L”) FO(ID) be the sequent calculus obtained from SC FO(ID) by replacing (def L) with (def L”) .The inference rule (def L) is admissible in SC (def L”) FO(ID) .

Proof. The following SC (def L”) FO(ID) -derivation shows that whenever the premises of an instantiation of (def L) are SC (def L”) FO(ID) -provable, then so is the conclusion: minor premises (def L”) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ], ΔΓ, Φ, 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ Δ (wk) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ), 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ Δ (cut) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ

□

3.4 Examples 

Example 3.8. Let Φ be the definition of Even from Example 2.6, let Γ denote the Peano axioms ∀𝑥, 𝑦 : succ (𝑥 ) =

Example 3.9. Let Φ be the definition of Sat from Example 2.7. Given Φ and some properties of the construction function not , we can formally prove that a structure J containing a propositional symbol 𝑄 does not satisfy ¬𝑄 .Let Γ denote the set of formulas Member (𝑞, 𝑗 ); ∀𝑓 , 𝑔 : not (𝑓 ) = not (𝑔 ) ⇒ 𝑓 = 𝑔 ; ∀𝑓 , 𝑔, ℎ : and (𝑓 , 𝑔 ) ≠ not (ℎ);and ∀𝑝, 𝑖, 𝑓 : Member (𝑝, 𝑖 ) ⇒ 𝑝 ≠ not (𝑓 ). Then we can prove the sequent Γ, Φ ⊢ ¬ Sat ( 𝑗, not (𝑞 )) as follows: minor premises Γ, Φ, 𝐹 Sat [ 𝑗, not (𝑞 )] ⊢ (def L) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑆𝑎𝑡 ( 𝑗, not (𝑞 )) ⊢ (¬R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ ¬ Sat ( 𝑗, not (𝑞 )) 

The minor premises take the following form: 

Γ, Φ, Member (𝑝, 𝑖 ) ⊢ 𝐹 Sat [𝑖, 𝑝 ]

Γ, Φ, 𝐹 Sat (𝑖, 𝑓 ) ∧ 𝐹 𝑆𝑎𝑡 [𝑖, 𝑔 ] ⊢ 𝐹 Sat [𝑖, and (𝑓 , 𝑔 )] 

Γ, Φ, ¬Sat (𝑖, 𝑓 ) ⊢ 𝐹 Sat [𝑖, not (𝑓 )] 

The induction hypothesis 𝐹 Sat needs to hold for every pair (𝑖, 𝑓 ) such that 𝑖 satisfies 𝑓 , but not for ( 𝑗, not (𝑞 )) . We can pick 𝐹 Sat [𝑖, 𝑓 ]  ∀ℎ : 𝑓 = not (ℎ) ⇒ ¬ Sat (𝑖, ℎ ), which says that if 𝑓 is the negation of a formula ℎ, then ℎ is 20 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

not satisfied by 𝑖 . The major premise is proven as follows: (=R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ not (𝑞 ) = not (𝑞 )

(ax) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Member (𝑞, 𝑗 ) (def R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Sat ( 𝑗, 𝑞 ) (¬L) 

Γ, Φ, ¬Sat ( 𝑗, 𝑞 ) ⊢ (⇒L) 

Γ, Φ, not (𝑞 ) = not (𝑞 ) ⇒ ¬ Sat ( 𝑗, 𝑞 ) ⊢ (∀L) 

Γ, Φ, 𝐹 Sat [ 𝑗, not (𝑞 )] ⊢ 

The proofs of the minor premises are straightforward, given the formulas in Γ.

Example 3.10. Let Φ be the definition of Active and ActivePath from Example 2.9. We can formally prove that if no edge of the form (𝑎, 𝑚 ) is ever activated, then all active edges remain active. We do so by proving the sequent 

Γ, Φ ⊢ ∀ 𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 : Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ⇒ Active (𝑛, 𝑚, succ (𝑡 )) , where Γ = {¬∃ 𝑚, 𝑡 : Activate (𝑎, 𝑚, 𝑡 )} :(ax) 

Γ, Φ, Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ⊢ Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 )

Γ, Φ, Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ), ActivePath (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑡 ) ⊢ (¬R) 

Γ, Φ, Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ⊢ ¬ ActivePath (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑡 ) (∧R) 

Γ, Φ, Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ⊢ Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ∧ ¬ ActivePath (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑡 ) (def R) 

Γ, Φ, Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ⊢ Active (𝑛, 𝑚, succ (𝑡 )) (⇒R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ⇒ Active (𝑛, 𝑚, succ (𝑡 )) (∀R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ ∀ 𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 : Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ⇒ Active (𝑛, 𝑚, succ (𝑡 )) 

We can derive the sequent Γ, Φ, Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ), ActivePath (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑡 ) ⊢ with (def L), picking Π = {ActivePath } and 

𝐹 ActivePath [𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ]  ∃𝑝 : Active (𝑛, 𝑝, 𝑡 ). This rule application involves two minor premises: 

Γ, Φ, Active (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ⊢ ∃ 𝑝 : Active (𝑛, 𝑝, 𝑡 )

Γ, Φ, ∃𝑝 : Active (𝑛, 𝑝, 𝑡 ) ∧ ∃ 𝑝 : Active (𝑚, 𝑝, 𝑡 ) ⊢ ∃ 𝑝 : Active (𝑛, 𝑝, 𝑡 )

both of which are proven trivially, as well as a major premise: 

Γ, Φ, ∃𝑝 : Active (𝑎, 𝑝, 𝑡 ) ⊢ 

We can prove this premise with ( ∃L) and (def L), picking Π = {Active } and 𝐹 Active [𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ]  ∃𝑠 : Activate (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑠 ).This rule application involves two minor premises: 

Γ, Φ, Activate (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑡 ) ∧ ¬ ActivePath (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑡 ) ⊢ ∃ 𝑠 : Activate (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑠 )

Γ, Φ, (∃ 𝑠 : Activate (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑠 )) ∧ ¬ ActivePath (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑡 ) ⊢ ∃ 𝑠 : Activate (𝑛, 𝑚, 𝑠 )

and one major premise: 

Γ, Φ, ∃𝑠 : Activate (𝑎, 𝑝, 𝑠 ) ⊢ 

all of which are proven trivially. 

Example 3.11. Let Φ be the definition of Distance from Example 2.8. We can formally prove that if a graph has edges (𝑎, 𝑏 ) and (𝑏, 𝑐 ), but no edge (𝑎, 𝑐 ), then the distance from 𝑎 to 𝑐 in this graph is two. This is done by proving the sequent Γ, Φ ⊢ Distance (𝑎, 𝑐, two ), where Γ consists of the assumptions on Edge : Edge (𝑎, 𝑏 ),A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions • 21 

Edge (𝑏, 𝑐 ), ¬Edge (𝑎, 𝑐 ); unique names axioms for 𝑎 , 𝑏 and 𝑐 : 𝑎 ≠ 𝑏 , 𝑏 ≠ 𝑐 , 𝑎 ≠ 𝑐 ,; and facts about succ and ≤ (e.g., 

two = succ (one ), one = succ (zero ) and ∀𝑛 : 𝑛 ≤ zero ⇒ 𝑛 = zero ): 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, succ (zero )) (=L) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, one ) (ax) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Edge (𝑏, 𝑐 ) (∧R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, one ) ∧ Edge (𝑏, 𝑐 ) (∃R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ ∃ 𝑧 : Distance (𝑎, 𝑧, one ) ∧ Edge (𝑧, 𝑐 )

Γ, Φ, 𝑚 ≤ one , Distance (𝑎, 𝑐, 𝑚 ) ⊢ (∧L) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑚 ≤ one ∧ Distance (𝑎, 𝑐, 𝑚 ) ⊢ (∃L) 

Γ, Φ, ∃𝑚 : 𝑚 ≤ one ∧ Distance (𝑎, 𝑐, 𝑚 ) ⊢ (¬R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ ¬∃ 𝑚 : 𝑚 ≤ one ∧ Distance (𝑎, 𝑐, 𝑚 ) (∧R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ (∃ 𝑧 : Distance (𝑎, 𝑧, one ) ∧ Edge (𝑧, 𝑐 )) ∧ ¬(∃ 𝑚 : 𝑚 ≤ one ∧ Distance (𝑎, 𝑐, 𝑚 )) (def R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Distance (𝑎, 𝑐, succ (one )) (=R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Distance (𝑎, 𝑐, two )

We can derive the sequent Γ, Φ ⊢ Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, succ (zero )) by (def R) from Γ, Φ ⊢ (∃ 𝑧 : Distance (𝑎, 𝑧, zero ) ∧ 

Edge (𝑧, 𝑏 )) ∧ ¬∃ 𝑚 : 𝑚 ≤ zero ∧ Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 ). The latter sequent can be derived by ( ∧R) from Γ, Φ ⊢ ∃ 𝑧 :

Distance (𝑎, 𝑧, zero ) ∧ Edge (𝑧, 𝑏 ) and Γ, Φ ⊢ ¬∃ 𝑚 : 𝑚 ≤ zero ∧ Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 ), which are proven as follows: (ax) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ ⊤ (def R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Distance (𝑎, 𝑎, zero ) (ax) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Edge (𝑎, 𝑏 ) (∧R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ Distance (𝑎, 𝑎, zero ) ∧ Edge (𝑎, 𝑏 ) (∃R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ ∃ 𝑧 : Distance (𝑎, 𝑧, zero ) ∧ Edge (𝑧, 𝑏 )

Γ, Φ, Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, zero ) ⊢ (=L) 

Γ, Φ, Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 ), 𝑚 = zero ⊢ (wk) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑚 ≤ zero , Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 ), 𝑚 = zero ⊢

(ax) 

𝑚 ≤ zero ⊢ 𝑚 ≤ zero (wk) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑚 ≤ zero , Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 ), 𝑚 ≤ zero ⊢ 𝑚 ≤ zero (⇒L) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑚 ≤ zero , Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 ), 𝑚 ≤ zero ⇒ 𝑚 = zero ⊢ (∀L) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑚 ≤ zero , Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 ) ⊢ (∧L) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑚 ≤ zero ∧ Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 ) ⊢ (∃L) 

Γ, Φ, ∃𝑚 : 𝑚 ≤ zero ∧ Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 ) ⊢ (¬R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ ¬∃ 𝑚 : 𝑚 ≤ zero ∧ Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑚 )

In the application of ( ∀L), we used the formula ∀𝑛 : 𝑛 ≤ zero ⇒ 𝑛 = zero in Γ. The sequent Γ, Φ, Distance (𝑎, 𝑏, zero ) ⊢ 

can be proven via (def L), picking 𝐹 Distance [𝑥, 𝑦, 𝑛 ]  (𝑛 = zero ⇒ 𝑥 = 𝑦 ). Similarly, the sequent Γ, Φ, 𝑚 ≤

one , Distance (𝑎, 𝑐, 𝑚 ) ⊢ in the first derivation can be proven via (def L), picking 𝐹 Distance [𝑥, 𝑦, 𝑛 ]  (𝑛 = zero ⇒

𝑥 = 𝑦 ) ∧ ( 𝑛 = one ⇒ Edge (𝑥, 𝑦 )) .

Example 3.12. Let Φ be the definition of Reachable from Example Example 2.2. By Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ], we denote the definition obtained from Φ by replacing Reachable with 𝑅 , and by Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ], we denote the definition obtained from 

Φ by replacing Reachable with 𝑄 and 𝑠 with 𝑡 . The defined predicate 𝑄 of Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ] represents the set of all nodes reachable from the node 𝑡 . We can prove that for all nodes 𝑠 , 𝑡 and 𝑟 , if 𝑡 is reachable from 𝑠 , and 𝑟 is reachable from 22 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

𝑡 , then 𝑟 is reachable from 𝑠 as well, by proving the sequent ⊢ ∀ 𝑠, 𝑡, 𝑟 : Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ] ∧ 𝑅 (𝑡 ) ∧ Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ] ∧ 𝑄 (𝑟 ) ⇒ 𝑅 (𝑟 ):14 

Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ], 𝑅 (𝑡 ), Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ], 𝑄 (𝑟 ) ⊢ 𝑅 (𝑟 ) (∧L) 

Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ] ∧ 𝑅 (𝑡 ) ∧ Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ] ∧ 𝑄 (𝑟 ) ⊢ 𝑅 (𝑟 ) (⇒R) 

⊢ Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ] ∧ 𝑅 (𝑡 ) ∧ Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ] ∧ 𝑄 (𝑟 ) ⇒ 𝑅 (𝑟 ) (∀R) 

⊢ ∀ 𝑠, 𝑡, 𝑟 : Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ] ∧ 𝑅 (𝑡 ) ∧ Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ] ∧ 𝑄 (𝑟 ) ⇒ 𝑅 (𝑟 )

(Here we merged three applications of ( ∧L) and of ( ∀R) in one.) To prove the sequent Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ], 𝑅 (𝑡 ), Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ], 𝑄 (𝑟 ) ⊢ 

𝑅 (𝑟 ), we apply (def L) w.r.t. Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ], picking 𝐹 𝑄 [𝑥 ]  𝑅 (𝑥 ). This yields the following premises: 

Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ], 𝑅 (𝑡 ), Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ] ⊢ 𝑅 (𝑡 )

Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ], 𝑅 (𝑡 ), Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ], 𝑅 (𝑥 ) ∧ 𝐸 (𝑥, 𝑦 ) ⊢ 𝑅 (𝑦 )

Φ[𝑠, 𝑅 ], 𝑅 (𝑡 ), Φ[𝑡, 𝑄 ], 𝑅 (𝑟 ) ⊢ 𝑅 (𝑟 )

Each of these sequents is trivially proven with (ax) and (def R). As illustrated by the following example, SC FO(ID) enables proving non-totality of definitions. 

Example 3.13. Let Φ be the definition {𝑃 ← ¬ 𝑃 }. We can formally prove that Φ is non-total by deriving its negation. Indeed, if ¬Φ is valid, then Φ has no two-valued models under the well-founded semantics. Hence, its well-founded model (in any trivial Φ-context) must be strictly three-valued. The main idea behind to proof is to show that Φ implies both the truth and the falsity of 𝑃 :(ax)

3.5 The Set Π

Proofs by induction generally involve multiple defined predicates. In particular, this is the case for mutually defined predicates, such as Even and Odd in the definition from Example 2.3. Proving that every even number is a natural number, for instance, is done by simultaneously proving that every odd number is a natural number. Formally, this is reflected by the set Π in (def L), which would be {Even , Odd } in this example. In general, to derive a sequent Γ, Φ, 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ⊢ Δ with (def L) in SC FO(ID) , the set Π can be any subset of def (Φ) containing 𝑃 .15 In LKID, the set Π is fixed to be the set consisting of all defined predicates that are mutually dependent with 𝑃 [9]. 

Definition 3.15. Let Φ be a definition and 𝑃, 𝑄 ∈ def (Φ). We say that 𝑃 depends directly on 𝑄 (w.r.t. Φ) if 𝑄 

occurs in the body 𝜑 of a definitional rule ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 of Φ. The direct dependency relation DDep Φ (w.r.t. Φ)

contains all pairs of defined predicates (𝑅, 𝑆 ) for which 𝑅 depends directly on 𝑆 . The (indirect) dependency relation 

Dep Φ (w.r.t. Φ) is the reflexive-transitive closure of the direct dependency relation DDep Φ w.r.t. Φ. We say that 

𝑃 depends (indirectly) on 𝑄 (w.r.t. Φ) if (𝑃, 𝑄 ) ∈ Dep Φ. We say that 𝑃 and 𝑄 are mutually dependent (w.r.t. Φ) if 

(𝑃, 𝑄 ) ∈ Dep Φ and (𝑄, 𝑃 ) ∈ Dep Φ. We denote by MD Φ (𝑃 ) the set of all defined predicates of Φ that are mutually dependent with 𝑃 w.r.t. Φ.As demonstrated by Example 3.10, it is not always necessary to include all mutually dependent predicates in Π.In this example, we proved a theorem about the definition Φ of Active and ActivePath from Example 2.9. Even though Active and ActivePath are mutually dependent w.r.t. Φ, the proof from Example 3.10 applied (def L) once with Π = {Active } and once with Π = {ActivePath }. Hence, the generality of Π in SC FO(ID) allows for smaller proofs. Proposition 3.18 guarantees that we can always restrict Π to a subset of MD Φ (𝑃 ).     

> 15 Here we mean that (def L) is sound (w.r.t. the well-founded, stable and Henkin semantics) for any choice of Π, in the sense that validity of the premises implies validity of the conclusion. However, one generally needs to choose Πwisely, as not all choices yield provable premises. 24 •Van den Eede and Denecker

Lemma 3.16. Let Γ and Δ be sets of FO(ID) -formulas, 𝜑 an FO(ID) -formula, 𝑥 an object symbol and 𝑦 an object symbol that does not occur freely in 𝜑 . Then Γ ⊢ ∀ 𝑥 : 𝜑, Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable iff Γ ⊢ ∀ 𝑦 : 𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ], Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable, and Γ, ∃𝑥 : 𝜑 ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable iff Γ, ∃𝑦 : 𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ] ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. 

Proof. If Γ ⊢ ∀ 𝑥 : 𝜑, Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable, then so is Γ ⊢ ∀ 𝑦 : 𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ], Δ, as can be seen from the following SC FO(ID) -derivation: 

Γ ⊢ ∀ 𝑥 : 𝜑, Δ (wk) 

Γ ⊢ ∀ 𝑥 : 𝜑, ∀𝑦 : 𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ], Δ

𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ] ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ] (∀L) 

∀𝑥 : 𝜑 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ] (∀R) 

∀𝑥 : 𝜑 ⊢ ∀ 𝑦 : 𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ] (wk) 

Γ, ∀𝑥 : 𝜑 ⊢ ∀ 𝑦 : 𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ], Δ (cut) 

Γ ⊢ ∀ 𝑦 : 𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ], Δ

In the application of ( ∀R), we used that 𝑦 does not occur freely in 𝜑 . The other direction follows by symmetry, as 

𝜑 [𝑦 /𝑥 ] [ 𝑥 /𝑦 ]  𝜑 . The proof of the second part of the statement is similar. □

Lemma 3.17. Let 𝜑 , 𝜓 and 𝜒 be FO -formulas and Γ and Δ sets of FO(ID) -formulas. Let 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +] denote a formula obtained from 𝜒 by substituting positive occurrences of 𝜑 (not necessarily all or any) by 𝜓 , and let 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ]

denote a formula obtained from 𝜒 by substituting negative occurrences of 𝜑 (again, not necessarily all or any) by 𝜓 .If Γ, 𝜑 ⊢ 𝜓, Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable, then so are Γ, 𝜒 ⊢ 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ and Γ, 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒, Δ.

Proof. See Appendix B. □

Proposition 3.18. Let SC ⊆MD FO(ID) be the sequent calculus obtained from SC FO(ID) by restricting Π in (def L) to subsets of MD Φ (𝑃 ). A sequent is SC FO(ID) -provable iff it is SC ⊆MD FO(ID) -provable. 

Proof. The “if” part is trivial, as every SC ⊆MD FO(ID) -proof is also an SC FO(ID) -proof. For the “only if” part, we take an SC FO(ID) -provable sequent and show by induction on the height of an SC FO(ID) -proof 𝑇 of the sequent that it is SC ⊆MD FO(ID) -provable as well. By Propositions 3.6 and 3.7, we can replace the rule (def L) by (def L”) in SC FO(ID) 

and SC ⊆MD FO(ID) .16 The base cases are trivial, and so are most cases in the inductive step. The only non-trivial case is where the sequent is derived by (def L”): minor premises (def L”) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ], Δ

The minor premises

Since they admit a smaller SC FO(ID) -proofs than 𝑇 , they are SC ⊆MD FO(ID) -provable by induction hypothesis. We show that Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ], Δ is SC ⊆MD FO(ID) -provable by induction over the dependency order ≺ on def (Φ), which is defined by 𝑄 ≺ 𝑅 iff (𝑅, 𝑄 ) ∈ Dep Φ and

4 Correspondence to SC FO 

The introduction rules for defined atoms in SC FO(ID) are based on two first-order properties of FO(ID)-definitions: the right introduction rule (def R) uses the fact that bodies imply heads, and the left introduction rule (def L) uses (a generalization of) the induction principle. In this section, we make these underlying first-order principles explicit by establishing a correspondence between SC FO(ID) -theorems and FO(ID)-theorems. In the FO(ID)-theorems, FO(ID)-definitions are replaced by their first-order approximations :Given a definition Φ, we denote by MI (Φ) the set of material implications ∀¯𝑥 : 𝜑 ⇒ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) corresponding to the definitional rules ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 of Φ. By IS (Φ), we denote the induction scheme corresponding to Φ. This is the (countably infinite) set of FO-formulas 

Û

> Π

(∀ ¯𝑦 : 𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⇒ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ]) ⇒ (∀ ¯𝑥 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑥 ) ⇒ 𝐹 𝑃 [ ¯𝑥 ]) 

where 𝑃 is a defined predicate of Φ, Π a subset of def (Φ) containing 𝑃 , and 𝐹 𝑄 [ ¯𝑦 ] is an FO-formula for every 

𝑄 ∈ Π. The conjunction ÓΠ (∀ ¯𝑦 : 𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⇒ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ]) ranges over all definitional rules ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 in Φ with 

𝑄 ∈ Π. The first-order approximation FO (Φ) of Φ is the set MI (Φ) ∪ IS (Φ).As a consequence of Propositions 2.19 and 2.22, any definition Φ logically entails its first-order approximation FO (Φ) under the well-founded semantics. Conversely, FO (Φ) does not always logically entail Φ. This is the case even for the positive definition of natural numbers ΦNat from Section 1. Indeed, non-standard models of Peano arithmetic satisfy FO (ΦNat ), but not ΦNat .

Lemma 4.1. Let Φ be a definition and 𝜒 ∈ FO (Φ). Then Φ ⊢ 𝜒 is SC FO(ID) -provable.

Φ, 𝜑 ⊢ 𝜑 (def R) 

Φ, 𝜑 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) (⇒R) 

Φ ⊢ 𝜑 ⇒ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) (∀R) 

Φ ⊢ ∀ ¯𝑥 : 𝜑 ⇒ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 )

as follows: minor premises (ax)

□

As with most of the subsequent proof-theoretical results, the correspondence theorem restricts to regular sequents . These are sequents of the form Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ, where Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 are definitions and Γ and Δ contain only FO-formulas. Even though non-regular sequents are generally sensible and useful (as illustrated in Examples 3.12, 3.13 and 3.14), many statements about inductive definitions are regular, as they are often of the form “given these definitions, the following property holds”. The correspondence theorem says that the SC FO(ID) -provability of a regular sequent Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ comes down to the SC FO -provability of the sequent Γ, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ) ⊢ Δ,obtained from Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ by replacing every definition Φ𝑖 by its first-order approximation FO (Φ𝑖 ). Note that Γ, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ) ⊢ Δ is a infinite sequent. As for finite sequents, an infinite sequent Γ ⊢ Δ is said to be 

SC -provable if there exists a (finite) SC -proof of Γ ⊢ Δ, for any sequent calculus SC .

Theorem 4.2 (Correspondence). Let Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ be a regular sequent. Then Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable iff Γ, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ) ⊢ Δ is SC FO -provable. 

Proof. Suppose that Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. Then it admits an SC FO(ID) -proof 𝑇 . We show that 

Γ, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ) ⊢ Δ is SC FO -provable by induction on the height of 𝑇 .In the base case, 𝑇 derives Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 , Γ ⊢ Δ by (ax) or (=R). This means that Γ ∩ Δ ≠ ∅ or ⊤ ∈ Δ or ⊥ ∈ Γ,or if Δ contains an atom of the form 𝑡 = 𝑡 . In either case, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ), Γ ⊢ Δ can be proven in SC FO 

by the same inference rule. For the inductive step, most cases are trivial. The only non-trivial cases are where

Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 , 𝑋 \ { 𝜒 } ⊢ 𝜒, Δ Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 , 𝑋 ⊢ Δ (cut) 

Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 , 𝑋 \ { 𝜒 } ⊢ Δ

Repeating this argument a finite number of times, we obtain that Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. □

The correspondence theorem characterizes the deductive strength of a definition Φ in SC FO(ID) by its first-order approximation FO (Φ) = MI (Φ) ∪ IS (Φ). Note that the induction scheme IS (Φ) corresponding to a definition Φ is generally weaker than the induction axioms Ind (Φ, Π) from Definition 2.20 for all Π ⊆ def (Φ). This is because the second-order variables in the induction axioms range over all relations over the domain, while the induction scheme restricts to the relations defined by FO-formulas. For an infinite domain 𝐷 , there are uncountably many relations over 𝐷 , but only countably many FO-definable ones. 

5 Alternative Semantics 5.1 Stable Semantics 

The induction rule of SC FO(ID) handles the non-monotonicity of FO(ID)-definitions in a surprisingly simple way: in the minor premises, only positive occurrences of defined predicates are replaced by an induction hypothesis, whereas negative occurrences remain untouched. While this may appear as an arbitrary and perhaps naive approach, it emerges naturally from the stable (model) semantics for logic programming [ 20 ]. This alternative to the well-founded semantics underlies the paradigm of answer set programming (ASP) [ 27 ]. Based on the presentation in [ 16 ], we will define the stable semantics for FO(ID), 19 allowing us to capture (fragments of) logic programs under the stable semantics in FO(ID). By its non-constructive nature, the stable semantics is not a semantics of inductive definitions. Furthermore, an FO(ID)-definition (or program) Φ generally has multiple stable models in a Φ-context, whereas a definition ought to provide a single interpretation of its defined predicates given an interpretation of its parameters. Nevertheless, the stable semantics coincides with the well-founded semantics on all ‘sensible’ definitions Φ and Φ-contexts O. More concretely, if the well-founded model I of a definition Φ in a Φ-context O is two-valued, then Φ has a unique stable model in O, which is precisely I.Henceforth, we fix an FO(ID)-definition Φ and a Φ-context O. The structure space S be the set of all sym (Φ)-structures expanding O. For I, J ∈ S , we write I ⊑ J if 𝑃 I ⊆ 𝑃 J for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). As can be easily checked, the relation ⊑ defines a complete lattice order on S. This means that any subset 𝐴 of S has a (unique) least upper bound and greatest lower bound, which we denote by ⊔𝐴 and ⊓𝐴 respectively. Let def (Φ)′ be a set that contains, for each 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ), a predicate symbol 𝑃 ′ with the same arity as 𝑃 that does not occur in Φ. Given 

I, J ∈ S , we define IJ as the expansion of I with vocabulary sym (Φ) ∪ def (Φ)′ that interprets every 𝑃 ′ as 𝑃 J

for every 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). In short, IJ = I [ def (Φ)′ : def (Φ) J ]. Given an FO-formula 𝜑 , we denote by 𝜑 ′ the formula obtained from 𝜑 by replacing all negative occurrences of defined predicates 𝑃 of Φ by 𝑃 ′. We define the operator 

C : S × S → S by 𝑃 C ( I , J ) ≔ { ¯𝑎 | I J [ ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑎 ] | = 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 )′ } for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ), where ¯𝑦 is an ar (𝑃 )-tuple of object symbols not occurring freely in the body 𝜑 of any definitional rule of Φ of the form ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 .20 Given 

J ∈ S , we denote by C(· , J ) the unary operator S → S : I ↦ → C(I , J ) . It is straightforward to verify that 

C(· , J ) is monotone , i.e., C(I 1, J ) ⊑ C(I 2, J ) if I1 ⊑ I 2 for all I1, I2 ∈ S . By the Knaster-Tarski theorem [ 36 ], this implies that C(· , J ) has a (unique) least fixpoint lfp (C(· , J )) , i.e., an I ∈ S such that I = C(I , J ) , and 

I ⊑ I ′ for every I′ ∈ S such that I′ = C(I ′, J ) . Furthermore, this least fixpoint is also the least pre-fixpoint     

> 19 Even though Denecker and Ternovska do not explicitly define the notion of stable model, they define the notion of stable operator , the fixpoints of which are called stable models.
> 20 We only need to define the interpretation of defined predicates, as the interpretation of parameters is fixed by O.A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions •29

of C(· , J ) , i.e., the least I ∈ S such that C(I , J ) ⊑ I . We define the stable operator ST : S → S by 

ST (I) ≔ lfp (C(· , I)) .

The fixpoints of ST are called the stable models of Φ in O. The stable satisfaction relation |=st is defined analogously to the well-founded satisfaction relation, the only difference being the rule: 

• I | =st Φ if I| sym (Φ) is a stable model of Φ in I| pars (Φ) .

Remark 5.1 . As stable models are not defined in terms of individual definitional rules, but only in terms of merged bodies 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ), any definition is equivalent to its normalization under the stable semantics. To make the link between SC FO(ID) and the stable semantics more explicit, let us unfold what it means for a structure I ∈ S to be a stable model of Φ in O. First, I needs to be a fixpoint of C(· , I) . This means that 𝑃 I = 𝑃 C ( I ,I ) = { ¯𝑎 | I [ ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑎 ] |= 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 )} for every 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). In other words, I needs to satisfy 

∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ⇔ 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) for every 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). Second, I needs to be the least fixpoint, or equivalently, pre-fixpoint, of C(· , I) . This means that for every structure J ∈ S , if C(J , I) ⊑ J , then I ⊑ J . The latter means that 

𝑃 I ⊆ 𝑃 J for every 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). The former means that { ¯𝑎 | J I [ ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑎 ] | = 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 )′ } = 𝑃 C ( J ,I ) ⊆ 𝑃 J for every 

𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). In JI [ ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] | = 𝜑 ′
