from Definition 2.20. Thus, the stable models of Φ in O are characterized precisely by Ind (Φ, def (Φ)) and the equivalences ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ⇔ 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ).By the reasoning in the previous paragraph, analogous versions of Propositions 2.19 and 2.22 hold for the stable semantics. However, we have only argued that an analogous version of 2.22 holds for Π = def (Φ). The result for general Π follows from the fact that I | =st Φ implies I | =st Φ′ if Φ′ is a subdefinition of Φ. This means that Φ′ is a subset of Φ such that, if Φ′ contains a definitional rule of Φ with 𝑃 in the head, it contains all definitional rules of 

Φ with 𝑃 in the head. We will prove this more generally for the Henkin semantics in Section 5.2. The well-founded model of Φ in O, as defined in Section 2.2, can also be defined in terms of the stable operator 

ST [ 16 , 17 ]. This is done by viewing three-valued structures I as pairs (I 𝑙 , I𝑢 ) of two-valued structures with 

I𝑙 ≤t I𝑢 . Here 𝜑 I = t iff 𝜑 I𝑙 = 𝜑 I𝑢 = t, 𝜑 I = f iff 𝜑 I𝑙 = 𝜑 I𝑢 = f, and 𝜑 I = u iff 𝜑 I𝑙 = f and 𝜑 I𝑢 = t for any formula 𝜑 interpreted by I. The well-founded model of Φ in O corresponds to the maximal oscillating pair of ST .This is the pair (I , J ) consisting of the ≤t-minimal I ∈ S and the ≤t-maximal J ∈ S such that ST (I) = J and 

ST (J ) = I. Equivalently, I is the ≤t-minimal and J the ≤t-maximal fixpoint of ST 2 ≔ ST ◦ ST . Translating a result by Van Gelder et al. [ 39 ] to the logic FO(ID), Denecker and Ternovska showed that if the well-founded model I of Φ in O is two-valued, there is precisely one stable model of Φ in O, which is I [16 ]. Hence, the well-founded and the stable semantics agree whenever Φ is total in O.

5.2 Henkin Semantics 

We generalize the stable semantics to the Henkin semantics for FO(ID). This semantics is based on the homonymous semantics for the inductive definition logic of Brotherston and Simpson [ 9 ], which is in turn inspired by Henkin’s semantics for second-order and higher-order logic [ 22 ]. In the Henkin semantics for FO(ID), a structure I is supplemented with a Henkin class H consisting of subsets of dom (I) 𝑘 for all 𝑘 ∈ N. The Henkin models of a definition Φ are then defined by restricting the structure space S to those sym (Φ)-structures that interpret all 30 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

defined predicates of Φ as relations in H . The sets in a Henkin class must be sufficiently rich. Given a set 𝐴 , we denote by P ( 𝐴 ) the power set of 𝐴 .

Definition 5.2. Let I be an FO-structure with domain 𝐷 . A Henkin class H for I is a set {𝐻 𝑘 | 𝑘 ∈ N} such that 𝐻 𝑘 ⊆ P ( 𝐷 𝑘 ) for all 𝑘 ∈ N, and (i) {( 𝑎, 𝑎 ) | 𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 } ∈ 𝐻 2;(ii) 𝑄 I ∈ 𝐻 ar (𝑄 ) for every predicate symbol 𝑄 in voc (I) ;(iii) if 𝑅 ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 +1 and 𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 , then {( 𝑎 1, . . . , 𝑎 𝑘 ) ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | ( 𝑎 1, . . . , 𝑎 𝑘 , 𝑎 ) ∈ 𝑅 } ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 ;(iv) if 𝑅 ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 , 𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 are terms, 𝑥 1, . . . , 𝑥 𝑛 , 𝑦 1, . . . , 𝑦 𝑚 are distinct object symbols and 𝑏 1, . . . , 𝑏 𝑚 ∈ 𝐷 , then 

{ ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑛 | ¯𝑡 I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] ∈ 𝑅 } ∈ 𝐻 𝑛 ;(v) if 𝑅 ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 , then 𝐷 𝑘 \ 𝑅 ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 ;(vi) if 𝑅 ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 and 𝑆 ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 , then 𝑅 ∩ 𝑆 ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 ;(vii) if 𝑅 ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 +1, then {( 𝑎 1, . . . , 𝑎 𝑘 ) ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | ( 𝑎 1, . . . , 𝑎 𝑘 , 𝑎 ) ∈ 𝑅 for all 𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 } ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 .A Henkin structure is a pair (I , H ) consisting of an FO-structure I and a Henkin class H for I.Henkin classes are sufficiently rich to interpret FO-formulas. This result will be used in establishing the soundness of LFO(ID) w.r.t. the Henkin semantics. 

Proposition 5.3. Let I be an FO -structure with domain 𝐷 and {𝐻 𝑘 | 𝑘 ∈ N} a Henkin class for I. Let 𝜑 be an 

FO -formula over voc (I) , 𝑥 1, . . . , 𝑥 𝑘 , 𝑦 1, . . . , 𝑦 𝑛 distinct variables and 𝑏 1, . . . , 𝑏 𝑛 ∈ 𝐷 . Then 

{ ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] | = 𝜑 } ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 .

Proof. See Appendix C. □

Conversely, one can obtain Henkin classes by collecting FO-definable sets .

Proposition 5.4. Let I be an FO -structure with domain 𝐷 . For every 𝑘 ∈ N, let 𝐻 𝑘 be the set of all sets of the 

{ ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] | = 𝜑 } such that 𝜑 is an FO -formula over voc (I) , ¯𝑥 and ¯𝑦 are tuples of distinct object symbols (not sharing any object symbol) and ¯𝑏 a tuple of elements of 𝐷 with the same length as ¯𝑦 . Then {𝐻 𝑘 | 𝑘 ∈ N}

is a Henkin class for I, which we call the Henkin class of FO-definable sets for I.

Proof. See Appendix C. □

Henceforth, we fix an FO(ID)-definition Φ and a Φ-context O. We define the structure space S, the relation ⊑

and the operator C as in Section 5.1. Let I ∈ S , and let H = {𝐻 𝑘 | 𝑘 ∈ N} be a Henkin class for I. We say that a structure J ∈ S is an H -point if 𝑃 J ∈ 𝐻 ar (𝑃 ) for every 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). We say that (I , H ) is a Henkin model of Φ

in O if I is the least pre-fixed H -point of C(· , I) (given that it exists). This means that I = C(I , I) , and that 

J = C(J , I) implies I ⊑ J for every H -point J . The Henkin satisfaction relation |=H in FO(ID) is a relation between Henkin structures and FO(ID)-formulas, defined by the following rules: 

• (I , H ) | =H 𝑡 = 𝑠 if 𝑡 I = 𝑠 I ;

• (I , H ) | =H 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) if ¯𝑡 I ∈ 𝑃 I ;

• (I , H ) | =H 𝜑 ∧ 𝜓 if (I , H ) | =H 𝜑 and (I , H ) | =H 𝜓 ;

• (I , H ) | =H ¬𝜑 if not (I , H ) | =H 𝜑 ;

• (I , H ) | =H ∀𝑥 : 𝜑 if (I [ 𝑥 : 𝑎 ], H ) | =H 𝜑 for all 𝑎 ∈ dom (I) ;

• (I , H ) | =H Φ if (I| sym (Φ) , H ) is a Henkin model of Φ in I| pars (Φ) .As before, the rules for ∨, ⇒, ⇔ and ∃ can de derived from the above rules through reformulation in terms of ¬,

∧ and ∀. Note that if 𝜑 is an FO-formula, then (I , H ) | =H 𝜑 comes down to I | = 𝜑 . Since the definition of Henkin model does not refer to individual definition rules, any FO(ID)-definition is equivalent to its normalization under the Henkin semantics. A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions • 31 

Any stable model I of Φ in O corresponds to a Henkin model (I , H ) of Φ in O, where H is the trivial Henkin class for I, i.e., the Henkin class {𝐻 𝑘 | 𝑘 ∈ N} where 𝐻 𝑘 = P ( 𝐷 𝑘 ) for all 𝑘 ∈ N. Indeed, if H is the trivial Henkin class for I, then the least pre-fixed H -point of C(· , I) comes down to the least (pre-)fixpoint of C(· , I) .Conversely, there generally exist Henkin models (I , H ) of Φ in O such that I is not a stable model of Φ in O.Brotherston derived the existence of such Henkin models for the natural number definition, using the existence of non-standard models of arithmetic [8]. 

Proposition 5.5. Let Φ be a definition, 𝑃 a defined predicate of Φ, and ¯𝑦 an ar (𝑃 )-tuple of object symbols not occurring freely in the body 𝜑 of any definitional rule of Φ of the form ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 . Let (I , H ) be a Henkin structure such that (I , H ) | =H Φ. Then I | = ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ⇔ 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ).

Proof. We can assume without loss of generality that voc (I) = sym (Φ). Since (I , H ) is a Henkin model of Φ, I = C(I , I) . This implies that 𝑃 I = 𝑃 C ( I ,I ) . By definition of C, this means that I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯𝑎 ] | = 𝑃 ( ¯𝑥 ) iff 

I [ ¯𝑦 : ¯ 𝑎 ] | = 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) for all ¯𝑎 . Thus, (I , H ) | =H ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ⇔ 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ). □

Corollary 5.6. Let Φ be a definition, 𝑃 a defined predicate of Φ, and ¯𝑦 an ar (𝑃 )-tuple of object symbols not occurring freely in the body 𝜑 of any definitional rule of Φ of the form ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 . Let I be an FO -structure such that I | =st Φ. Then I | = ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ⇔ 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ).

Proof. This follows from Proposition 5.5 by taking H to be the trivial Henkin class for I. □

Proposition 5.7. Let (I , H ) be an Henkin structure, Φ a definition and Φ′ a subdefinition of Φ. If (I , H ) | =H Φ,then (I , H ) | =H Φ′.

Proof. See Appendix C. □

6 Soundness 

In this section, we show that our sequent calculus SC FO(ID) is sound w.r.t. the well-founded, the stable and the Henkin semantics. This means that every SC FO(ID) -theorem is valid under all three semantics. Since SC FO(ID) 

is sound w.r.t. the well-founded and the stable semantics, it can be used to reason about (fragments of) logic programs under both semantics. 

Theorem 6.1 (Soundness). SC FO(ID) is sound with respect to the well-founded, the stable and the Henkin semantics. 

Proof. Let Γ ⊢ Δ be an SC FO(ID) -theorem. Then Γ ⊢ Δ admits an SC FO(ID) -proof 𝑇 . We prove by induction on the height of 𝑇 that Γ ⊢ Δ is valid under the Henkin semantics. The reasoning for the well-founded and the stable semantics is similar. We only discuss the cases where 𝑇 derives Γ ⊢ Δ by (def R) or (def L), as the cases corresponding to the other inference rules are covered by the soundness of SC FO :21 

Case : the final inference in 𝑇 is of the form 

Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ], Δ (def R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ]) , Δ

By induction hypothesis, Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ], Δ is valid under the Henkin semantics. Let (I , H ) be a Henkin structure, and assume that (I , H ) | =H

Ó Γ ∧Φ. Since (I , H ) | =H Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ], Δ, this implies that (I , H ) | =H 𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ] ∨ Ô Δ.If (I , H ) | =H 𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ], then I | =H 𝑃 (¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ]) , by Proposition 5.5 and Definition 2.11. If (I , H )̸ | =H 𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ], then

(I , H ) | =H ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑥 ) ⇒ 𝐹 𝑃 [ ¯𝑥 ]. Since (I , H ) | =H 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ), we find that (I , H ) | =H 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ]. Since (I , H ) | =H

The fact that SC FO(ID) is sound with respect to all three semantics provides insights into the limitations of SC FO(ID) , as it cannot prove statements on which the semantics disagree.

Example 6.2. The definition 

Φ =

 𝑃 ← ¬ 𝑄 𝑄 ← ¬ 𝑃

has no two-valued well-founded models, but it has two (two-valued) stable models: one in which 𝑃 is true and 

𝑄 is false, and one in which 𝑃 is false and 𝑄 is true. Hence, even though ⊢ ¬ Φ is valid under the well-founded semantics, it is not SC FO(ID) -provable, as it is not valid under the stable semantics. 

7 Completeness 

The converse property of soundness is completeness , which says that any valid formula is provable. SC FO(ID) is not complete w.r.t. the well-founded or the stable semantics. This is the case even when restricting to positive definitions, for which both the well-founded and the stable semantics boil down to the standard least fixpoint semantics. The incompleteness is a consequence of Gödel’s first incompleteness theorem, as SC FO(ID) admits a canonical theory of the natural numbers, by capturing Peano arithmetic. More specifically, this theory contains two FO-formulas expressing the unique names axioms for zero /0 and succ /1:

∀𝑛 : succ (𝑛 ) ≠ zero , ∀𝑛, 𝑚 : succ (𝑛 ) = succ (𝑚 ) ⇒ 𝑠 = 𝑚, 

a definition and an FO-formula expressing the induction scheme: 

 Nat (zero )∀𝑛 : Nat (succ (𝑛 )) ← Nat (𝑛 )



, ∀𝑛 : Nat (𝑛 ),

and four FO-formulas defining the arithmetic operators plus and times :

∀𝑛 : plus (succ (𝑛 ), zero ) = succ (𝑛 ), ∀𝑛, 𝑚 : plus (succ (𝑛 ), 𝑚 ) = succ (plus (𝑛, 𝑚 )) ,

∀𝑛 : times (succ (𝑛 ), zero ) = zero , ∀𝑛, 𝑚 : times (succ (𝑛 ), 𝑚 ) = plus (times (𝑛, 𝑚 ), 𝑛 ).

Although completeness w.r.t. the well-founded or the stable semantics is unachievable, we can still get a theoretical grasp on the deductive strength of SC FO(ID) by proving weaker completeness results. We do so by proving completeness of a propositional fragment of FO(ID) w.r.t. the stable semantics, and by proving completeness of a first-order order fragment of SC FO(ID) w.r.t. the Henkin semantics. 

7.1 Propositional Completeness 

In Section 4, we saw that any definition Φ logically entails its first-order approximation FO (Φ) under the well-founded semantics, but that FO (Φ) does not always logically entail Φ. By Propositions 5.5 and 5.8 and Corollaries 5.6 and 5.9, the same is true for the stable and the Henkin semantics. When restricting to propositional definitions ,i.e., definitions in which all predicates are 0-ary, FO (Φ) logically entails Φ under the stable semantics. 22 

Lemma 7.1. Let Φ be a propositional definition. Then FO (Φ) logically entails Φ under the stable semantics. 

Proof. Let I be an FO-structure such that I | =st FO (Φ). Without loss of generality, we can assume that 

voc (I) = sym (Φ), and that Φ is in normal form. To prove that I | =st Φ, we need to show that I is the least pre-fixpoint of CΦ (· , I) . Since I | =st MI (Φ), I is a pre-fixpoint of CΦ (· , I) . Indeed, for any defined predicate 𝑃 of 

Φ, we have that 𝑃 CΦ ( I ,I ) = 𝜑 I 

> 𝑃

≤t 𝑃 I , as I | =st 𝜑 𝑃 ⇒ 𝑃 . Since I | =st IS (Φ), I is the least pre-fixpoint of CΦ (· , I) .Indeed, suppose that J is a pre-fixpoint of CΦ (· , I) , i.e., CΦ (J , I) ⊑ J . Let 𝑃 be a defined predicate of Φ. For every defined predicate 𝑄 of Φ, let 𝐺 𝑄  ⊤ if J | = 𝑄 and 𝐺 𝑄  ⊥ if J̸ | = 𝑄 . Then I | =st 

Ó𝑄 ∈Π (𝜑 𝑄 [𝐺 Π/Π+] ⇒ 𝐺 𝑄 ).Since I | =st 

Ó𝑄 ∈Π (𝜑 𝑄 [𝐺 Π/Π+] ⇒ 𝐺 𝑄 ) ⇒ ( 𝑃 ⇒ 𝐺 𝑃 ), it follows that I | =st 𝑃 ⇒ 𝐺 𝑃 . This means that 𝑃 I ≤t 𝑃 J .Since 𝑃 was chosen arbitrarily, this shows that I ⊑ J . □

Theorem 7.2 (Propositional completeness). Let Γ and Δ be sets of propositional formulas and Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 

propositional definitions. If Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is valid under the stable semantics, then it is SC FO(ID) -provable. 

Proof. Suppose that Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is valid under the stable semantics. By Lemma 7.1, 

Γ, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ) ⊢ Δ is valid in FO. By completeness of SC FO , we obtain that Γ, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ) ⊢ Δ is SC FO -provable. By Theorem 4.2, Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. □

As shown by Example 6.2, propositional completeness does not hold under the well-founded semantics. Indeed, for the definition Φ of this example, the sequent Φ ⊢ is valid under the well-founded semantics, but it is not SC FO(ID) -provable (as it is not valid under the stable semantics). 

7.2 Henkin Completeness 

The Henkin completeness result for SC FO(ID) relies on the following lemma, which provides a relation between the first-order approximations of definitions and Henkin classes of FO-definable sets. 

Lemma 7.3. Let Φ be a definition, I an FO -structure, and H the Henkin class of FO -definable sets for I. If 

I | = FO (Φ), then (I , H ) | =H Φ.

Proof. Without loss of generality, we can assume that voc (I) = sym (Φ), and that Φ is in normal form. We show that I is the least pre-fixed H -point of CΦ (· , I) . Trivially, I is an H -point. The fact that I is a pre-fixpoint of CΦ (· , I) follows from the fact that I | = MI (Φ). Indeed, for any defined predicate 𝑃 of Φ, I | =

∀¯𝑦 : 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ⇒ 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ), and therefore, 𝑃 CΦ ( I ,I ) = { ¯𝑎 | I [ ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑎 ] | = 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 )} ⊆ { ¯𝑎 | I [ ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑎 ] | = 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 )} = 𝑃 I . Here ¯𝑦 

is an ar (𝑃 )-tuple of object symbols not occurring freely in the body of any definitional rule of Φ of the form 

∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 . The fact that I is the least pre-fixed H -point of CΦ (· , I) follows from the fact that I | = IS (Φ).Indeed, let J be a pre-fixed H -point of CΦ (· , I) . Since J is an H -point, there exists, for every 𝑄 ∈ def (Φ),an FO-formula 𝐹 𝑄 and an ar (𝑄 ) tuple of object symbols ¯𝑧 such that 𝑄 J = { ¯𝑏 | I [ ¯𝑧 : ¯𝑏 ] | = 𝐹 𝑄 }. For every 

𝑄 ∈ def (Φ), the fact that 𝑄 CΦ ( J ,I ) ⊆ 𝑄 J can be restated as I | = ∀¯𝑦 : 𝜑 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) [ 𝐹 def (Φ) /def (Φ)+] ( ¯𝑦 ) ⇒ 𝐹 𝑄 [ ¯𝑦 ].Since I | =

Ó𝑄 ∈def (Φ) ∀¯𝑦 : 𝜑 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) [ 𝐹 def (Φ) /def (Φ)+] ⇒ 𝐹 𝑄 [ ¯𝑦 ]



⇒ ∀ ¯𝑥 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑥 ) ⇒ 𝐹 𝑃 [ ¯𝑥 ], this implies that I | = ∀¯𝑥 :

𝑃 ( ¯𝑥 ) ⇒ 𝐹 𝑃 [ ¯𝑥 ] for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). Stated differently, 𝑃 I ⊆ 𝑃 J for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ), which means that I ⊑ J . This shows that I is indeed the least pre-fixed H -point of CΦ (· , I) . □

Theorem 7.4 (Henkin completeness). Let Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ be a regular sequent. If Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is valid under the Henkin semantics, then it is SC FO(ID) -provable. 

Proof. Suppose that Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is valid under the Henkin semantics. Then Γ, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ) ⊢ Δ

is valid in FO. Indeed, let I be an FO-structure such that I | = Ó Γ ∧ Ó FO (Φ1) ∧ · · · ∧ Ó FO (Φ𝑛 ). Let H be the Henkin class of FO-definable sets for I. By Lemma 7.3, (I , H ) | =H

Ó Γ ∧ Φ1 ∧ · · · ∧ Φ𝑛 . By assumption, 

(I , H ) | =H

Ô Δ, and since Δ contains only FO-formulas, I | =H

Ô Δ. This shows that Γ, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ) ⊢ Δ

is indeed valid in FO. By completeness of SC FO , we obtain that Γ, FO (Φ1), . . . , FO (Φ𝑛 ) ⊢ Δ is SC FO -provable. By Theorem 4.2, Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. □

8 Cut-elimination 

Brotherston and Simpson showed that cut-elimination holds for LKID [ 9 ], meaning that every LKID-provable sequent can be proven without the cut rule. From the perspective of proof search, cut-elimination has limited relevance in induction-based proof systems when compared to first-order systems. This is because, unlike in first-order proof systems, cut-elimination in induction-based systems does not entail the subformula property .This property states that one may restrict to analytic inference rules, in which the formulas in the premises are subformulas of the formulas in the conclusion. In induction-based systems like LKID and SC FO(ID) , the A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions • 35 

subformula property is averted by the induction rule, as the induction hypotheses in the premises do not appear as subformulas in the conclusion. Therefore, when following a bottom-up approach (i.e., from conclusion to premises), the induction hypotheses are unconstrained by the conclusion, meaning that they can take the form of any FO-formula. Cut-elimination is nevertheless meaningful in induction-based systems, as it entails that unconstrained formulas need only occur as induction hypotheses, and not as lemmas [8]. As demonstrated by Example 3.13, cut-elimination does not hold in SC FO(ID) . Indeed, from a simple inspection of all inference rules of SC FO(ID) , one can see that no rule other than (cut) applies to derive the sequent  𝑃 ← ¬ 𝑃 ⊢.Three more counterexamples are included below: 

Example 8.1. Let Φ =  𝑃 ← 𝑂 ∨ ¬ 𝑃 . We can prove the sequent Φ ⊢ 𝑂 as follows: (ax) 

Φ, ¬𝑃 ⊢ 𝑂, ¬𝑃 (∨R) 

Φ, ¬𝑃 ⊢ 𝑂 ∨ ¬ 𝑃 (def R) 

Φ, ¬𝑃 ⊢ 𝑃 (¬R) 

Φ ⊢ 𝑃, ¬¬ 𝑃 (∗)

Φ ⊢ 𝑃 (wk) 

Φ ⊢ 𝑃, 𝑂 

(ax) 

Φ, 𝑂 ∨ ¬ 𝑃 ⊢ 𝑂 ∨ ¬ 𝑃, 𝑂 

(ax) 

Φ, 𝑂 ⊢ 𝑂 

(ax) 

Φ, ¬𝑃 ⊢ 𝑂, ¬𝑃 (∨R) 

Φ, ¬𝑃 ⊢ 𝑂 ∨ ¬ 𝑃 (def R) 

Φ, ¬𝑃 ⊢ 𝑃 (¬R) 

Φ ⊢ 𝑃, ¬¬ 𝑃 (∗)

Φ, 𝑃 ⊢ 𝑂 (cut) 

Φ ⊢ 𝑂 

We used the induction hypothesis 𝐹 𝑃  𝑂 ∨ ¬ 𝑃 in the application of (def L). The transition indicated by ( ∗) is completed as the homonymous transition in Example 3.13. The sequent Φ ⊢ 𝑂 can only be proven via (cut), as can be checked by a simple inspection of the inference rules of SC FO(ID) .

Example 8.2. Let Φ =

 𝑃 ← 𝑂 𝑃 ← ¬ 𝑂 



. Then we can prove the sequent Φ ⊢ 𝑃 as follows: (ax) 

Φ, 𝑂 ⊢ 𝑂 (¬R) 

Φ ⊢ 𝑂, ¬𝑂 (def R) 

Φ ⊢ 𝑂, 𝑃 

(ax) 

Φ, 𝑂 ⊢ 𝑂 (def R) 

Φ, 𝑂 ⊢ 𝑃 (cut) 

Φ ⊢ 𝑃 

The sequent cannot be proven without (cut), as can be checked by going through all inference rules. Indeed, the only other rule that can derive Φ ⊢ 𝑃 is (def R), either from the premise Φ ⊢ 𝑂 or from the premise Φ ⊢ ¬ 𝑂 .However, neither of these sequents is SC FO(ID) -provable, as neither is valid (under any of the three semantics). As can be easily checked, the sequent ΦN ⊢ 𝑃 is provable without (cut). This shows that Proposition 3.3 does not extend to provability without cut. 

Example 8.3. Consider the definitions Φ1 =  𝑃 ← 𝑄 and Φ2 =  𝑃 ← 𝑅 . We can prove the sequent 

Φ1, Φ2, 𝑄 ⊢ 𝑅 as follows: 23 

(ax) 

Φ1, Φ2, 𝑄 ⊢ 𝑄, 𝑅 (def R) 

Φ1, Φ2, 𝑄 ⊢ 𝑃, 𝑅 

(ax) 

Φ1, Φ2, 𝑄, 𝑅 ⊢ 𝑅 (ax) 

Φ1, Φ2, 𝑄, 𝑅 ⊢ 𝑅 (def L) 

Φ1, Φ2, 𝑄, 𝑃 ⊢ 𝑅 (cut) 

Φ1, Φ2, 𝑄 ⊢ 𝑅   

> 23 This example comes from [25]. 36 •Van den Eede and Denecker

We used the induction hypothesis 𝐹 𝑃  𝑅 in the application of (def L). As before, a simple inspection of all inference rules of SC FO(ID) shows that Φ1, Φ2, 𝑄 ⊢ 𝑅 can only be proven via (cut). Even though cut-elimination does not hold for general sequents in SC FO(ID) , we can identify a fragment of sequents for which it holds. One option is the fragment of sequents Γ, Φ ⊢ Δ such that Γ and Δ are finite sets of FO-formulas, and Φ is a definition in which the body of every definitional rule is a conjunction of atoms. Cut-elimination holds for this fragment because it corresponds to the logic of inductive definitions considered by Brotherston and Simpson [ 9]. In Theorem 8.6, we establish cut-elimination for a slightly broader fragment of FO(ID)-sequents Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ, containing multiple definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 , in which the bodies of the rules may also contain disjunctions. The definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 must be such that no two definitions Φ𝑖 , Φ𝑗 define the same predicate, and the defined predicates of a definition Φ𝑖 do not occur in a preceding definition Φ𝑗 .

Definition 8.4. Let Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ be a regular sequent. We say that Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is canonical if Γ and Δ

are finite, and for all 𝑖, 𝑗 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }:(i) the body of each definitional rule of Φ𝑖 is composed of literals by conjunctions and disjunctions; (ii) def (Φ𝑖 ) ∩ def (Φ𝑗 ) = ∅ if 𝑖 ≠ 𝑗 ;(iii) def (Φ𝑖 ) ∩ pars (Φ𝑗 ) = ∅ if 𝑗 < 𝑖 .To prove Theorem 8.6, we follows the semantic approach by Brotherston and Simpson, who proved cut-elimination for LKID by proving cut-free Henkin completeness for LKID. This means that all sequents that are valid under the Henkin semantics are provable without the cut rule. Combined with Henkin soundness, cut-free Henkin completeness implies cut-elimination. We say that an FO(ID)-sequent is cut-free provable (in SC FO(ID) ) if it admits an SC FO(ID) -proof without (cut). 

Theorem 8.5 (Cut-free Henkin completeness). Let Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ be a canonical sequent such that no negation occurs in the body of any definitional rule of any definition Φ𝑖 . If Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is valid under the Henkin semantics, then it is cut-free provable. 

The proof of Theorem 8.5 is analogous to the proof of cut-free Henkin completeness for LKID [ 9], and is included in Appendix D.1. The rough outline of the proof is as follows: (i) Assume that Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is not cut-free provable. (ii) We construct (infinite) sets of FO-formulas Γ𝜔 and Δ𝜔 , extending Γ and Δ, respectively, such that 

Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 is not cut-free provable. (iii) We construct a Henkin counter-model for Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 , i.e., a Henkin structure (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) such that 

(I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) | =H 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Γ𝜔 , (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) | =H Φ𝑖 for all 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 } and (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 )̸ | =H 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Δ𝜔 . In particular, we have that (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) | =H 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Γ and (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 )̸ | =H 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Δ.(iv) Hence, Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is not valid under the Henkin semantics. By contraposition, Theorem 8.5 holds. 

Theorem 8.6 (Cut-elimination). Let Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ be a canonical sequent such that no negation occurs in the body of any definitional rule of any definition Φ𝑖 . If Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is provable, then it is cut-free provable. 

Proof. If Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is provable, then it is valid under the Henkin semantics by Theorem 6.1, whence it is cut-free provable by Theorem 8.5. □

As shown by Example 8.2, we cannot extend Theorems 8.5 and 8.6 to allow for negation in the bodies of definitional rules, not even in front of parameters. Nevertheless, we will establish a weakened version of cut-elimination for canonical sequents Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ in which the bodies of the rules of the definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 

contain negation, as long as they are stratified (Theorem 8.14). In this weakened version, cuts are allowed, but only on formulas of the form ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) ∨ ¬ 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) such that 𝑄 appears negatively in the body of a rule of a definition 

Φ𝑖 , and ¯𝑦 is a fixed ar (𝑄 )-tuple of object symbols. We say that a canonical sequent Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is provable A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions • 37 

with elementary cuts (in SC FO(ID) ) if there exists an SC FO(ID) -proof of Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ in which every cut formula is of this form. This weakened version of cut-elimination, which we call cut-restriction , is valuable for proof search, as it narrows the options for a cut formula down to a finite list. Before we prove the result for stratified definitions, we prove it for definitions in which negation occurs only in front of parameters (Lemma 8.9). Our strategy is to transform a sequent Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ with definitions of this form to a semantically equivalent sequent Γ, Ω, Φc1, . . . , Φc 

> 𝑛

⊢ Δ with positive definitions Φc1, . . . , Φc 

> 𝑛

by means of auxiliary predicates, and to apply Theorem 8.5 to Γ, Ω, Φc1, . . . , Φc 

> 𝑛

⊢ Δ.

We now generalize the previous lemma to canonical sequents with stratified definitions. We do so by decom-posing the stratified definitions into monotone subdefinition. 

Definition 8.10. Let Φ be a stratified definition. A decomposition of Φ is a tuple (Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑘 ) of subdefinitions of Φ such that the definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑘 form a partitioning of Φ, and there exists a stratification ℓ of Φ such that for all 𝑖, 𝑗 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑘 }, 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ) and 𝑄 ∈ def (Φ𝑗 ): ℓ (𝑃 ) < ℓ (𝑄 ) iff 𝑖 < 𝑗 . We say that the stratification ℓ

corresponds to the decomposition (Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑘 ).

Remark 8.11 . It is straightforward to see that every stratified definition Φ admits a decomposition (Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑘 ),and that ℓ (𝑃 ) = ℓ (𝑄 ) for all 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑘 } and all 𝑃, 𝑄 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ), where ℓ is a stratification of Φ corresponding to the decomposition (Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑘 ). Furthermore, only parameter predicates occur negatively in the bodies of rules

of definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 . Indeed, if a defined predicate 𝑄 of a definition Φ𝑖 were to occur negatively in the body 

𝜑 of a definitional rule ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 of Φ𝑖 , then, using the fact that 𝑃 and 𝑄 are both defined by Φ𝑖 , we would deduce that ℓ (𝑄 ) < ℓ (𝑃 ) = ℓ (𝑄 ); a contradiction. 

Lemma 8.12. Let Φ be a stratified definition, (Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑘 ) a decomposition of Φ, and (I , H ) a Henkin structure. If 

(I , H ) | =H Φ𝑖 for all 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑘 }, then (I , H ) | =H Φ.

Proof. Suppose that (I , H ) | =H Φ𝑖 for all 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑘 }. Assume without loss of generality that voc (I) =

sym (Φ). We need to show that I is the least pre-fixed H -point of CΦ (· , I) . Since H is a Henkin class for I, I is trivially an H -point. To show that I is a pre-fixed point of CΦ (· , I) , pick a defined predicate 𝑃 of Φ. Let Φ𝑖 be the positive subdefinition of Φ defining 𝑃 . Since 𝜑 𝑃, Φ ( ¯𝑦 )  𝜑 𝑃, Φ𝑖 ( ¯𝑦 ) for every ar (𝑃 )-tuple of object symbols ¯𝑦 , we have that 𝑃 CΦ ( I ,I ) = 𝑃 CΦ𝑖 ( I ,I ) . Since (I , H ) | =H Φ𝑖 , it follows that 𝑃 CΦ ( I ,I ) = 𝑃 CΦ𝑖 ( I ,I ) ⊆ 𝑃 I . This shows that indeed CΦ (I , I) ⊑ I .We now show that I is the least pre-fixed H -point of CΦ (· , I) . Let J be a pre-fixed H -point of CΦ (· , I) . Let 𝑃 

be a defined predicate of Φ, and let Φ𝑖 be the positive subdefinition of Φ that defines 𝑃 . It is straightforward to check that J | sym (Φ𝑖 ) is a pre-fixed H -point of CΦ𝑖 (· , I| sym (Φ𝑖 ) ). Since (I , H ) | =H Φ𝑖 , the structure I| sym (Φ𝑖 ) is the least pre-fixed H -point of CΦ𝑖 (· , I| sym (Φ𝑖 ) ), which implies that I| sym (Φ𝑖 ) ⊑ J | sym (Φ𝑖 ) . Hence, 𝑃 I = 𝑃 I | sym (Φ𝑖 ) ⊆

𝑃 J | sym (Φ𝑖 ) = 𝑃 J . This shows that J ⊑ I , and thus, that I is the least pre-fixed H -point of CΦ (· , I) . □

Theorem 8.13. Let Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ be a canonical sequent such that the definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 are stratified. If 

Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is valid under the Henkin semantics, then it is provable with elementary cuts. 

Proof. Suppose that Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is valid under the Henkin semantics. For each 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }, let 

(Φ𝑖, 1, . . . , Φ𝑖,𝑘 𝑖 ) be a decomposition of Φ𝑖 . Consider Γ, Φ1,1, . . . , Φ1,𝑘 1 , . . . , Φ𝑛,𝑘 𝑛 ⊢ Δ. By Lemma 8.12, this sequent is valid under the Henkin semantics. Furthermore, Γ, Φ1,1, . . . , Φ1,𝑘 1 , . . . , Φ𝑛,𝑘 𝑛 ⊢ Δ satisfies the conditions of Lemma 8.9. Indeed, it is a canonical sequent, as for all 𝑖, 𝑖 ′ ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }, 𝑗 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑘 𝑖 } and 𝑗 ′ ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑘 𝑖 ′ }:(1) def (Φ𝑖,𝑗 )∩ def (Φ𝑖 ′,𝑗 ′ ) = ∅ if 𝑖 ≠ 𝑖 ′ or if 𝑗 ≠ 𝑗 ′. If, 𝑖 ≠ 𝑖 ′, this follows from the fact that def (Φ𝑖 )∩ def (Φ𝑖 ′ ) = ∅,and if 𝑖 = 𝑖 ′ and 𝑗 ≠ 𝑗 ′, from the fact that different subdefinitions of a definition define different predicates. (2) def (Φ𝑖,𝑗 ) ∩ pars (Φ𝑖 ′,𝑗 ′ ) = ∅ if 𝑖 ′ < 𝑖 or if 𝑖 ′ = 𝑖 and 𝑗 ′ < 𝑗 . If 𝑖 ′ < 𝑖 , this follows from the fact that 

def (Φ𝑖 ) ∩ pars (Φ𝑖 ′ ) = ∅, and if 𝑖 ′ = 𝑖 and 𝑗 ′ < 𝑗 , from Definition 8.10. Indeed, suppose that a defined predicate 𝑃 of Φ𝑖,𝑗 occurred as a parameter of Φ𝑖,𝑗 ′ for some 𝑗 ′ < 𝑗 . Then 𝑃 would occur in the body 𝜓 of a definitional rule ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 of Φ𝑖,𝑗 ′ . However, letting ℓ be a stratification of Φ𝑖 corresponding to the decomposition (Φ𝑖, 1, . . . , Φ𝑖,𝑘 𝑖 ), this would imply that ℓ (𝑃 ) ≤ ℓ (𝑄 ) < ℓ (𝑃 ); a contradiction. Furthermore, by Remark 8.11, only parameter predicates occur negatively in the bodies of rules of definitions Φ𝑖,𝑗 .Thus, Lemma 8.9 applies, which yields that Γ, Φ1,1, . . . , Φ1,𝑘 1 , . . . , Φ𝑛,𝑘 𝑛 ⊢ Δ is provable with elementary cuts. By induction on the height of a cut-free proof of Γ, Φ1,1, . . . , Φ1,𝑘 1 , . . . , Φ𝑛,𝑘 𝑛 ⊢ Δ, it follows that Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is also provable with elementary cuts. Here we used that any application of (def L) w.r.t. a definition Φ𝑖,𝑗 is also an application of (def L) w.r.t. Φ𝑖 . □

Theorem 8.14 (Cut-restriction). Let Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ be a canonical sequent such that the definitions 

Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 are stratified. If Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is provable, then it is provable with elementary cuts. 

Proof. If Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is provable, then it is valid under the Henkin semantics by Theorem 6.1, whence it is provable with elementary cuts by Theorem 8.13. □

Note that Theorems 8.13 and 8.14 generalize Theorems 8.5 and 8.6, respectively, since, if the bodies of the rules of the definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 contain no negation, then there are no elementary cuts. The counterexamples from the start of this section leave open the possibility that cut-restriction holds for a broader fragment of sequents than the one described in Theorem 8.14. Indeed, it is straightforward to check that A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions • 39 

the sequents in Examples 3.13, 8.1 and 8.2 are provable with elementary cuts. 25 Hence, the stratification condition on the definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 in Theorem 8.13 can plausibly be relaxed. Moreover, it is straightforward to check that the sequent Φ ⊢ 𝑃 from Example 8.2 is cut-free provable if we replace (def R) with the following alternative right introduction rule for defined atoms: 26 

Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ), Δ (def R’) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ), Δ

or alternatively, that the sequent ΦN ⊢ 𝑃 with the normalization ΦN =  𝑃 ← 𝑂 ∨ ¬ 𝑂 of Φ is cut-free provable in SC FO(ID) . This raises the possibility that cut-restriction can be strengthened to cut-elimination when replacing (def R) with (def R’) in SC FO(ID) , or when restricting to normal definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 . Thus, there are still multiple open questions in identifying fragments of FO(ID)-sequents that admit cut-restriction and cut-elimination. 

9 Conclusion 

We have introduced a sequent calculus SC FO(ID) for the logic FO(ID), thus enabling formal proofs about general inductive definitions. Remarkably, our sequent calculus supports proofs of non-totality, thereby offering formal tools to demonstrate that certain definitions are ill-formed. SC FO(ID) extends the sequent calculus LKID by Brotherston and Simpson, formalizing the principle of mathematical induction [ 9 ]. While covering a substantially larger class of inductive definitions, our sequent calculus remains syntactically close to LKID, the most fundamental difference being the asymmetry between positive and negative occurrences of defined atoms in the induction rule. We motivated our choice for this extended induction rule by linking it to the stable semantics, which is closely related to the well-founded semantics. To corroborate our calculus, we demonstrated it on various examples and established multiple proof-theoretical results for it. This paper paves the way for several directions for future research: 

• As remarked at the end of Section 8, the examples from the start of the section leave open the possibility that cut-restriction holds for a broader fragment than the one described in Theorem 8.14. Furthermore, the cut-restriction result for the sequents Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ in this fragment may be strengthened to cut-elimination by replacing (def R) by (def R’) in SC FO(ID) , or by restricting to normal definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 .

• Following the intuition from Section 3 of viewing induction hypotheses as upper bounds on defined predicates, we could refine our induction rule by substituting the negative occurrences of defined predicates with formulas describing a lower bound on these predicates. Such a rule could be stronger than our current induction rule. On the other hand, this refined rule would be more complex, requiring the choice of an additional kind of “induction hypothesis”, it would presumably not be sound w.r.t. the stable semantics, and it would deviate further from the principle of mathematical induction as used in practice. 

• Hou Ping and Denecker enhanced the deductive strength of their sequent calculus for FO(ID) by including least fixpoint expressions, thereby enlarging the class of expressible sets (while also introducing an infinitary inference rule) [ 24 ]. One could investigate whether the same principle applies to SC FO(ID) , as the addition of least fixpoint expressions would enlarge the class of expressible induction hypotheses. 

• Brotherston and Simpson also devised an infinitary and a cyclic sequent calculus for their logic of inductive definitions, in which proofs can take the form of infinite trees and graphs, respectively [ 9]. These calculi can be seen as formalizations of the principle of infinite descent. Compared to LKID, the main advantage of these calculi is that they do not require the use of induction hypotheses. Furthermore, both calculi are strictly stronger than LKID, i.e., their set of theorems is a strict superset of the set of LKID-theorems [ 4 ]. 

> 25 The sequent in Example 8.3 is not provable with elementary cuts (as it does not admit any elementary cuts), which shows that there are still limitations on cut-restriction in SC FO(ID) .
> 26 It is not difficult to show that (def R) and (def R’) are interchangeable, i.e., that replacing (def R) by (def R’) does not affect the set of theorems.

40 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

A straightforward question is whether this infinitary and this cyclic counterpart to LKID can be extended to FO(ID) as well. 

• Even though SC FO(ID) admits proofs of non-totality, it does not admit proofs of totality of definitions. Such proofs would nevertheless be valuable, as they show why certain definitions construct well-defined relations. In fact, enabling proofs of totality would require extending the language of FO(ID), as it is currently not possible to express that a definition is total in FO(ID). 

• Besides sequent calculi, one could also develop natural deduction systems for FO(ID). Natural deduc-tion systems are typically less susceptible to theoretical analysis than sequent calculi, but their proofs correspond more closely to mathematical practice. 

• One could extend SC FO(ID) to extensions of FO(ID) with additional language constructs, such as types (or sorts), aggregates, partial functions and modal operators. 

• SC FO(ID) could be implemented in proof assistants and proof logging algorithms, to enable interactive theorem proving and proof logging in FO(ID), respectively.

A Proof of Proposition 2.22 

Proposition 2.22. Let Φ be a definition and Π a subset of def (Φ). Let I be an FO -structure such that I | =wf Φ.Then I | = Ind (Φ, Π).

To prove Proposition 2.22, we associate with each three-valued structure 𝔄 a pair of two-valued structures 

(𝔄 𝑙 , 𝔄 𝑢 ). Specifically, we define 𝔄 𝑙 and 𝔄 𝑢 to have the same vocabulary and domain 𝐷 as 𝔄 , the same inter-pretation of non-predicate symbols, and for each predicate symbol 𝑃 , 𝑃 𝔄 𝑙 

≔ { ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 ar (𝑃 ) | 𝑃 𝔄 ( ¯𝑎 ) = t} and 42 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

𝑃 𝔄 𝑢 

≔ { ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 ar (𝑃 ) | 𝑃 𝔄 ( ¯𝑎 ) = t or 𝑃 𝔄 ( ¯𝑎 ) = u}. Intuitively, 𝔄 𝑙 and 𝔄 𝑢 can be seen as two-valued approxima-tions of 𝔄 by interpreting all unknown facts as false and as true respectively. It is straightforward to see that 

𝔄 𝑙 ≤t 𝔄 ≤t 𝔄 𝑢 , when viewing 𝔄 𝑙 and 𝔄 𝑢 as three-valued structures, and furthermore that 𝔄 ≤p 𝔅 if and only if 

𝔄 𝑙 ≤t 𝔅 𝑙 and 𝔅 𝑢 ≤t 𝔄 𝑢 for all three-valued structures 𝔄 and 𝔅 . We define a satisfaction relation |= between pairs (I , J ) of two-valued structures with the same domain and the same vocabulary, and FO-formulas 𝜑 . We do so by structural induction on 𝜑 , via the following rules: 

• (I , J ) | = 𝑡 = 𝑠 if 𝑡 I = 𝑠 I ;

• (I , J ) | = 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) if ¯𝑡 I ∈ 𝑃 I ;

• (I , J ) | = ¬𝜓 if (J , I)̸ | = 𝜓 ;

• (I , J ) | = 𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 if (I , J ) | = 𝜓 and (I , J ) | = 𝜒 ;

• (I , J ) | = ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 if (I [ 𝑥 : 𝑎 ], J [ 𝑥 : 𝑎 ]) | = 𝜓 for all 𝑎 ∈ dom (I) .The rules for ∨, ⇒, ⇔ and ∃ can de derived from the above rules through reformulation in terms of ¬, ∧ and 

∀. Under this definition, all positive occurrences of predicate symbols in 𝜑 are evaluated by I and all negative occurrences by J . Intuitively, one can read (I , J ) | = 𝜑 as saying that 𝜑 is certainly true when viewing I as a lower bound and J as an upper bound on what is known (i.e., what is true in J is possibly true and what is false in J is certainly false) [ 40 ]. We will write 𝜑 ( I , J ) = t if (I , J ) | = 𝜑 , and 𝜑 ( I , J ) = f if (I , J )̸ | = 𝜑 . For a merged body 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ), we write 𝜑 ( I , J )

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ¬𝜓 . If (¬ 𝜓 )𝔄 ∈ { f, t}, then also 𝜓 𝔄 ∈ { f, t}. By induction hypothesis, 𝜓 𝔄 = 𝜓 (𝔄 𝑙 ,𝔄 𝑢 ) =

and (𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 ) (𝔄 𝑢 ,𝔄 𝑙 ) = 𝜓 (𝔄 𝑢 ,𝔄 𝑙 ) ∧ 𝜒 (𝔄 𝑢 ,𝔄 𝑙 ) = f.If (𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 )𝔄 = t, then 𝜓 𝔄 = t and 𝜒 𝔄 = t. By induction hypothesis, 𝜓 (𝔄 𝑙 ,𝔄 𝑢 ) = 𝜓 (𝔄 𝑢 ,𝔄 𝑙 ) = 𝜓 𝔄 = t and

Cases : 𝜑 is of the form 𝜓 ∨ 𝜒 , 𝜓 ⇒ 𝜒 , or 𝜓 ⇔ 𝜒 . These cases are similar to the previous case.

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ∃𝑥 : 𝜓 . This case is similar to the previous case. □

Proposition A.2. Let I, I′, J and J ′ be two-valued structures with the same domain, vocabulary, and interpre-tation of non-predicate symbols. Let 𝜑 be an FO -formula. If I ≤ t I′ and J ′ ≤t J , then 𝜑 ( I , J ) ≤t 𝜑 ( I ′, J′ ) and 

𝜑 ( J ′,I′ ) ≤t 𝜑 ( J ,I ) .

Proof. We show by structural induction on 𝜑 that 𝜑 ( I , J ) ≤t 𝜑 ( I ′, J′ ) and 𝜑 ( J ′,I′ ) ≤t 𝜑 ( J ,I ) for all two-valued structures I, I′, J and J ′ with the same domain, vocabulary, and interpretation of non-predicate symbols, such that I ≤ t I′ and J ′ ≤t J .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form 𝑡 = 𝑠 . Since I, I′, J and J ′ have the same interpretation of terms, (𝑡 = 𝑠 )I = (𝑡 =

𝑠 )I′

= (𝑡 = 𝑠 ) J = (𝑡 = 𝑠 ) J′

, and hence, (𝑡 = 𝑠 ) ( I , J ) = (𝑡 = 𝑠 ) ( I ′, J′ ) = (𝑡 = 𝑠 ) ( J ,I ) = (𝑡 = 𝑠 ) ( J ′,I′ ) . In particular, 

(𝑡 = 𝑠 ) ( I , J ) ≤t (𝑡 = 𝑠 ) ( I ′, J′ ) and (𝑡 = 𝑠 ) ( J ′,I′ ) ≤t (𝑡 = 𝑠 ) ( J ,I ) .

Case 𝜑 is of the form 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ). If I ≤ t I′ and J ′ ≤t J , then 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ( I , J ) = 𝑃 (¯𝑡 )I ≤t 𝑃 (¯𝑡 )I′

= 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ( I ′, J′ ) and 

𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ( J ′,I′ ) = 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) J′

≤t 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) J = 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ( J ,I ) .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ¬𝜓 . Suppose that I ≤ t I′ and J ′ ≤t J . By induction hypothesis, 𝜓 ( I , J ) ≤t 𝜓 ( I ′, J′ )

and 𝜓 ( J ′,I′ ) ≤t 𝜓 ( J ,I ) . It follows that ¬𝜓 ( I ′, J′ ) ≤t ¬𝜓 ( I , J ) and ¬𝜓 ( J ,I ) ≤t ¬𝜓 ( J ′,I′ ) . Using the fact that 

(¬ 𝜒 ) ( K ,L) = ¬𝜒 ( L ,K ) for all FO-formulas 𝜒 and all pairs of two-valued structures (K , L) , we deduce that

min ≤t {𝜓 ( I ′, J′ ) , 𝜒 ( I ′, J′ ) } = (𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 ) ( I ′, J′ ) and (𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 ) ( J ′,I′ ) = min ≤t {𝜓 ( J ′,I′ ) , 𝜒 ( J ′,I′ ) }≤t min ≤t {𝜓 ( J ,I ) , 𝜒 ( J ,I ) } = (𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 ) ( J ,I ) .

Cases : 𝜑 is of the form 𝜓 ∨ 𝜒 , 𝜓 ⇒ 𝜒 , or 𝜓 ⇔ 𝜒 . These cases are similar to the previous case. 

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 . Suppose that I ≤ t I′ and J ′ ≤t J . Let 𝐷 be the domain of I, I′, J

and J ′. Then I [ 𝑥 : 𝑎 ] ≤ t I′ [𝑥 : 𝑎 ] and J ′ [𝑥 : 𝑎 ] ≤ t J [ 𝑥 : 𝑎 ] for all 𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 . By induction hypothesis, 

𝜓 ( I [ 𝑥 :𝑎 ], J [ 𝑥 :𝑎 ] ) ≤t 𝜓 ( I ′ [𝑥 :𝑎 ], J′ [𝑥 :𝑎 ] ) and 𝜓 ( J ′ [𝑥 :𝑎 ],I′ [𝑥 :𝑎 ] ) ≤t 𝜓 ( J [ 𝑥 :𝑎 ],I [ 𝑥 :𝑎 ] ) for all 𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 . It follows that (∀ 𝑥 :

𝜓 ) ( I , J ) = min ≤t {𝜓 ( I [ 𝑥 :𝑎 ], J [ 𝑥 :𝑎 ] ) | 𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 } ≤ t min ≤t {𝜓 ( I ′ [𝑥 :𝑎 ], J′ [𝑥 :𝑎 ] ) | 𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 } = (∀ 𝑥 : 𝜓 ) ( I ′, J′ ) and (∀ 𝑥 :

𝜓 ) ( J ′,I′ ) = min ≤t {𝜓 ( J ′ [𝑥 :𝑎 ],I′ [𝑥 :𝑎 ] ) | 𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 } ≤ t min ≤t {𝜓 ( J [ 𝑥 :𝑎 ],I [ 𝑥 :𝑎 ] ) | 𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 } = (∀ 𝑥 : 𝜓 ) ( J ,I ) .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ∃𝑥 : 𝜓 . This case is similar to the previous case. □

Proof of Proposition 2.22. We can assume without loss of generality that voc (I) = sym (Φ), so that I is the well-founded model of Φ in I| pars (𝜑 ) , and that Φ is normal. Let 𝐴 Π be a tuple of sets 𝐴 𝑄 ⊆ 𝐷 ar (𝑄 ) for every

B Proofs of Section 3 

Proposition 3.3. Let Γ and Δ be sets of FO(ID) -formulas and let Φ be a definition. Let ΦN be the normalization of Φ. Then Γ, Φ ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable iff Γ, ΦN ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. 

Proof. Suppose that Γ, Φ ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. We show that Γ, ΦN ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable by induction on the height of an SC FO(ID) -proof 𝑇 of Γ, Φ ⊢ Δ. The base cases are trivial, and so are most cases in the inductive step. We only discuss the interesting cases, which are those where Γ, Φ ⊢ Δ is derived by (def R) or (def L) in 𝑇 .

Case : Γ, Φ ⊢ Δ is derived by (def R): 

Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ], Δ (def R) 

Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ]) , Δ

Then Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ], Δ admits an SC FO(ID) -proof of height smaller than 𝑇 . By induction hypothesis, Γ, ΦN ⊢

𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ], Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. By application of ( =R) and ( ∧R), the sequent Γ, ΦN ⊢ ¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ] = ¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ] ∧ 𝜑 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ], Δ

is SC FO(ID) -provable as well, and by application of ( ∃R), so is Γ, ΦN ⊢ ∃ ¯𝑥 : ¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ] = ¯𝑡 ∧ 𝜑, Δ. (Here we assumed that none of the object symbols in ¯𝑥 occur in ¯𝑠 . We can rename the object symbols ¯𝑥 if this is not the case.) Since ∃ ¯𝑥 : ¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ] = ¯𝑡 ∧ 𝜑 is one of the disjuncts in 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ]) , repeated application of (wk) and ( ∨R) yields that Γ, ΦN ⊢ 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ]) , Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. Finally, since ΦN contains a rule ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ← 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ), the sequent 

Γ, ΦN ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 [¯𝑠 / ¯𝑥 ]) , Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable with (def R). 

Case : Γ, Φ ⊢ Δ is derived by (def L): minor premises Γ, Φ, 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ Δ (def L) 

Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ

The minor premises Γ, Φ,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ correspond to the definitional rules ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 of Φ with 

𝑄 ∈ Π. The premises admit SC FO(ID) -proofs of height smaller than 𝑇 . By induction hypothesis, the sequents 

Γ, ΦN,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ corresponding to the definitional rules ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 of Φ with 𝑄 ∈ Π are SC FO(ID) -provable, and so is Γ, ΦN, 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ Δ. Applying ( =L), ( ∧L), ( ∃L) and ( ∨L) to the former sequents, we find that 

Γ, ΦN, 𝜑 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) [ 𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable for every 𝑄 ∈ Π. Applying (def L) to these sequents and to 

Γ, ΦN, 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ Δ, we find that Γ, ΦN, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable as well. Suppose conversely that Γ, ΦN ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. We show that Γ, Φ ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable by induction on the height of an SC FO(ID) -proof 𝑇 of Γ, ΦN ⊢ Δ. Again, we restrict to the non-trivial cases, which are those where Γ, ΦN ⊢ Δ is derived by (def R) or (def L) in 𝑇 .A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions • 45 

Case : Γ, ΦN ⊢ Δ is derived by (def R). Since the definitional rules of ΦN are of the form ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ) ← 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ), the final inference in 𝑇 is of the form: Γ, ΦN ⊢ 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ (def R) 

Γ, ΦN ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ

Then Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ admits an SC FO(ID) -proof of height smaller than 𝑇 . By induction hypothesis, 

Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ (18) is SC FO(ID) -provable. It suffices to show that 

Γ, Φ, 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ) ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ (19) is SC FO(ID) -provable. Indeed, if this is the case, then applying (wk) and (cut) to (18) and (19) yields that Γ, Φ ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ

is SC FO(ID) -provable. To show that (19) is SC FO(ID) -provable, it suffices to show that Γ, Φ, ∃ ¯𝑥 : ¯𝑠 = ¯𝑡 ∧ 𝜑 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable for every rule ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 in Φ, using Definition 2.11 and ( ∨L). This can be done as follows: (ax) 

Γ, Φ, 𝜑 [ ¯𝑧 / ¯𝑥 ] ⊢ 𝜑 [¯𝑧 / ¯𝑥 ], Δ (def R) 

Γ, Φ, 𝜑 [ ¯𝑧 / ¯𝑥 ] ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 [ ¯𝑧 / ¯𝑥 ]) , Δ (=L) 

Γ, Φ, ¯𝑠 = ¯𝑡 [ ¯𝑧 / ¯𝑥 ], 𝜑 [¯𝑧 / ¯𝑥 ] ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ (∧L) 

Γ, Φ, ¯𝑠 = ¯𝑡 [ ¯𝑧 / ¯𝑥 ] ∧ 𝜑 [ ¯𝑧 / ¯𝑥 ] ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ (∃L) 

Γ, Φ, ∃ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑠 = ¯𝑡 ∧ 𝜑 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ), Δ

Here ¯𝑧 is a tuple of object symbols that do not occur freely in Γ, Δ or Φ, with the same length as ¯𝑥 . (We again assumed that none of the object symbols in ¯𝑥 occur in ¯𝑠 . We can rename ¯𝑥 if this is not the case.) 

Case : Γ, ΦN ⊢ Δ is derived by (def L): minor premises Γ, ΦN, 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ Δ (def L) 

Γ, ΦN, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ

The minor premises Γ, ΦN, 𝜑 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) [ 𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [ ¯𝑦 ], Δ correspond to the defined predicates 𝑄 ∈ Π. The premises admit SC FO(ID) -proofs of height smaller than 𝑇 . By induction hypothesis, the sequents 

Γ, Φ, 𝜑 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) [ 𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [ ¯𝑦 ], Δ (20) corresponding to the predicates 𝑄 ∈ Π are SC FO(ID) -provable, and so is 

Γ, Φ, 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑣 ] ⊢ Δ. (21) Let ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 be a definitional rule of Φ with 𝑄 ∈ Π. Consider the proof: (=R) 

𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ ¯𝑠 = ¯𝑠 (ax) 

𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] (∧R)

Γ, Φ,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝜑 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) [ 𝐹 Π/Π+], 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ (22) is SC FO(ID) -provable. Applying (subst) and (wk) to (20), we also find that 

Γ, Φ,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π+], 𝜑 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) [ 𝐹 Π/Π+] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ (23) is SC FO(ID) -provable. Applying (cut) to (22) and (23), we find that

is SC FO(ID) -provable. Finally, applying (def L) to (24) for all 𝑄 ∈ Π and to (21), we find that Γ, Φ, 𝑃 ( ¯𝑣 ) ⊢ Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable. □

Lemma 3.17. Let 𝜑 , 𝜓 and 𝜒 be FO -formulas and Γ and Δ sets of FO(ID) -formulas. Let 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +] denote a formula obtained from 𝜒 by substituting positive occurrences of 𝜑 (not necessarily all or any) by 𝜓 , and let 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ]

denote a formula obtained from 𝜒 by substituting negative occurrences of 𝜑 (again, not necessarily all or any) by 𝜓 .If Γ, 𝜑 ⊢ 𝜓, Δ is SC FO(ID) -provable, then so are Γ, 𝜒 ⊢ 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ and Γ, 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒, Δ.

Proof. We show the statement by structural induction on 𝜒 .

Case : 𝜒 is an atom. Then 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ]  𝜒 , and hence Γ, 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒, Δ can be derived by (ax). If 𝜒  𝜑 , then 

Γ, 𝜒 ⊢ 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ comes down to Γ, 𝜑 ⊢ 𝜓, Δ, which is provable by assumption. Otherwise, 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +]  𝜒 , and 

Γ, 𝜒 ⊢ 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ can be derived by (ax). 

Case : 𝜒 is of the form ¬𝜒 ′. Then 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +] is of the form ¬𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] for an FO-formula 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] obtained from 𝜒 ′ by replacing negative occurrences of 𝜑 by 𝜓 . By induction hypothesis, Γ, 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 ′, Δ is provable. We can then derive Γ, 𝜒 ⊢ 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ as follows: 

Γ, 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 ′, Δ (¬R) 

Γ ⊢ ¬ 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ], 𝜒 ′, Δ (¬L) 

Γ, ¬𝜒 ′ ⊢ ¬ 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ], Δ

The formula 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] is of the form ¬𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +] for an FO-formula 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +] obtained from 𝜒 ′ by replacing positive occurrences of 𝜑 by 𝜓 . By induction hypothesis, Γ, 𝜒 ′ ⊢ 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ is provable. We can then derive 

Γ, 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒, Δ as follows: 

Γ, 𝜒 ′ ⊢ 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ (¬R) 

Γ ⊢ ¬ 𝜒 ′, 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ (¬L) 

Γ, ¬𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +] ⊢ ¬ 𝜒 ′, Δ

Case : 𝜒 is of the form 𝜒 1 ∧ 𝜒 2. Then 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +] is of the form 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 +] ∧ 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 +] for FO-formulas 

𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 +] and 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 +] obtained from 𝜒 1 and 𝜒 2, respectively, by replacing positive occurrences of 𝜑 by 

𝜓 . By induction hypothesis, Γ, 𝜒 1 ⊢ 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ and Γ, 𝜒 2 ⊢ 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ are provable. We can then derive 

Γ, 𝜒 ⊢ 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ as follows: 

Γ, 𝜒 1 ⊢ 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ (wk) 

Γ, 𝜒 1, 𝜒 2 ⊢ 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 +], ΔΓ, 𝜒 2 ⊢ 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ (wk) 

Γ, 𝜒 1, 𝜒 2 ⊢ 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ (∧R) 

Γ, 𝜒 1, 𝜒 2 ⊢ 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 +] ∧ 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ (∧L) 

Γ, 𝜒 1 ∧ 𝜒 2 ⊢ 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 +] ∧ 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ

The formula 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] is of the form 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ∧ 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] for FO-formulas 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] and 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 − ]

obtained from 𝜒 1 and 𝜒 2, respectively, by replacing negative occurrences of 𝜑 by 𝜓 . By induction hypothesis, 

Γ, 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 1, Δ and Γ, 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 2, Δ are provable. We can then derive Γ, 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒, Δ as follows: 

Γ, 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 1, Δ (wk) 

Γ, 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 − ], 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 1, ΔΓ, 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 2, Δ (wk) 

Γ, 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 − ], 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 2, Δ (∧R) 

Γ, 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 − ], 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 1 ∧ 𝜒 2, Δ (∧L) 

Γ, 𝜒 1 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ∧ 𝜒 2 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 1 ∧ 𝜒 2, Δ

Cases : 𝜒 is of the form 𝜒 1 ∨ 𝜒 2, 𝜒 1 ⇒ 𝜒 2 or 𝜒 1 ⇔ 𝜒 2. These cases are similar to the previous case. A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions • 47 

Case : 𝜒 is of the form ∀𝑥 : 𝜒 ′. Then 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +] is of the form ∀𝑥 : 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +] for an FO-formula 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +]

obtained from 𝜒 ′ by replacing positive occurrences of 𝜑 by 𝜓 . By induction hypothesis, Γ, 𝜒 ′ ⊢ 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ is provable. We can then derive Γ, 𝜒 ⊢ 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ as follows: 

Γ, 𝜒 ′ ⊢ 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ (∀L) 

Γ, ∀𝑥 : 𝜒 ′ ⊢ 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ (∀R) 

Γ, ∀𝑥 : 𝜒 ′ ⊢ ∀ 𝑥 : 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +], Δ

In the application of ( ∀R), we assumed that 𝑥 does not occur freely in Γ or Δ, which is justified by Lemma 3.16. Indeed, if necessary, we can replace 𝑥 in ∀𝑥 : 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 +] by an object symbol 𝑦 that does not occur freely in 𝜑 , Γ

or Δ (and such object symbol exists, as Γ and Δ are finite). The formula 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] is of the form ∀𝑥 : 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] for an FO-formula 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] obtained from 𝜒 ′ by replacing negative occurrences of 𝜑 by 𝜓 . By induction hypothesis, Γ, 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 ′, Δ is provable. We can then derive Γ, 𝜒 [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒, Δ as follows: 

Γ, 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 ′, Δ (∀L) 

Γ, ∀𝑥 : 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ 𝜒 ′, Δ (∀R) 

Γ, ∀𝑥 : 𝜒 ′ [𝜓 𝜑 − ] ⊢ ∀ 𝑥 : 𝜒 ′, Δ

Again, we assumed that 𝑥 does not occur freely in 𝜑 , Γ or Δ, which is justified by Lemma 3.16. 

Case : 𝜒 is of the form ∃𝑥 : 𝜒 ′. This case is similar to the previous case. □

C Proofs of Section 5 

Proposition 5.3. Let I be an FO -structure with domain 𝐷 and {𝐻 𝑘 | 𝑘 ∈ N} a Henkin class for I. Let 𝜑 be an 

FO -formula over voc (I) , 𝑥 1, . . . , 𝑥 𝑘 , 𝑦 1, . . . , 𝑦 𝑛 distinct variables and 𝑏 1, . . . , 𝑏 𝑛 ∈ 𝐷 . Then 

{ ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] | = 𝜑 } ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 .

(𝑏 1, . . . , 𝑏 𝑛 ) ∈ 𝐷 𝑛 :

{ ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] | = 𝑡 = 𝑠 } = { ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | ( 𝑡 I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ], 𝑠 I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] ) ∈ 𝑅 } ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form 𝑄 (¯𝑡 ). Let 𝑘 denote the arity of 𝑄 . By condition (ii), 𝑄 I ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 . By condition (iv), 

{ ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] | = 𝑄 (¯𝑡 )} = { ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | ¯𝑡 I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] ∈ 𝑄 I } ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ¬𝜓 . By induction hypothesis, { ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] | = 𝜓 } ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 . By condition (v),

𝜓 } ∩ { ¯𝑎 ∈ 𝐷 𝑘 | I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯ 𝑎, ¯𝑦 : ¯𝑏 ] | = 𝜓 } ∈ 𝐻 𝑘 .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ∃𝑥 : 𝜓 . Using the fact that ∃𝑥 : 𝜓 is FO-equivalent to ¬∀ 𝑥 : ¬𝜓 , this follows from the previous cases. □48 • Van den Eede and Denecker

Proposition 5.7. Let (I , H ) be an Henkin structure, Φ a definition and Φ′ a subdefinition of Φ. If (I , H ) | =H Φ,then (I , H ) | =H Φ′.

Proof. We can assume without loss of generality that voc (I) = sym (Φ). Suppose that (I , H ) | =H Φ. Then 

I is the least pre-fixed H -point of C(· , I) . Let I′ ≔ I| sym (Φ′ ) . We need to show that I′ is the least pre-fixed H -point of C(· , I′). Since sym (Φ′) ⊆ sym (Φ), I′ is an H -point. Since I is a pre-fixpoint of C(· , I) ,

𝑃 I ⊆ { ¯𝑎 | I [ ¯𝑥 : ¯𝑎 ] | = 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑥 )} for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). It follows that 𝑃 I′

⊆ { ¯𝑎 | I ′ [ ¯𝑥 : ¯𝑎 ] | = 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑥 )} for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ′),which means that I′ is a pre-fixpoint of C(· , I′).27 

Let J ′ be a pre-fixed H -point of C(· , I′). Consider the sym (Φ)-structure J that interprets any 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ′)

as 𝑃 J′

∩ 𝑃 I , and any 𝜎 ∈ sym (Φ) \ def (Φ′) as 𝜎 I . Note that J ⊑ I and J | sym (Φ′ ) ⊑ J ′. We will show that J

is a pre-fixed H -point of C(· , I) . Since I is the least pre-fixed H -point of C(· , I) , this will imply that I ⊑ J ,and hence, that I′ = I| sym (Φ′ ) ⊑ J | sym (Φ′ ) ⊑ J ′, showing that I′ is the least pre-fixed H -point of C(· , I′). The

fact that J is an H -point follows from the fact that J ′ and I are H -points, using condition (vi) of Definition 5.2. To show that J is a pre-fixpoint of C(· , I) , take 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ).

Case : 𝑃 ∉ def (Φ′). Then 𝑃 C ( J ,I ) ⊆ 𝑃 C ( I ,I ) ⊆ 𝑃 I = 𝑃 J , using the fact that C(· , I) is monotone, and that I

D Proofs of Section 8 D.1 Proof of Theorem 8.5 

We prove Theorem 8.5: 

Theorem 8.5 (Cut-free Henkin completeness). Let Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ be a canonical sequent such that no negation occurs in the body of any definitional rule of any definition Φ𝑖 . If Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is valid under the Henkin semantics, then it is cut-free provable. 

Let Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ be as above. We will prove Theorem 8.5 by contraposition, i.e., we will show that if Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is not cut-free provable, then it is not valid under the Henkin semantics. Assume that 

Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is not cut-free provable. We will construct a limit sequent Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 , where Γ𝜔 and Δ𝜔 

are infinite sets of FO-formulas, containing Γ and Δ, respectively, such that Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 is not cut-free provable. Next, we will construct a Henkin counter-model for Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 , i.e., a Henkin structure (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 )

such that (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) | =H 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Γ𝜔 , (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) | =H Φ𝑖 for all 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }, but (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 )̸ | =H 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Δ𝜔 .Let Σ be the vocabulary consisting of all non-logical symbols in Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ, and let Terms (Σ) be the set of all terms built from function symbols in Σ. We construct the limit sequent Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 via a schedule .

Definition D.1. A schedule element is defined by the following rules: 

• If 𝜑 and 𝜓 are FO-formulas over Σ, then ¬𝜑 , 𝜑 ∧ 𝜓 , 𝜑 ∨ 𝜓 , 𝜑 ⇒ 𝜓 and 𝜑 ⇔ 𝜓 are schedule elements. 

• If 𝜑 is an FO-formula over Σ, 𝑥 an object symbol in Σ, and 𝑡 ∈ Terms (Σ), then ⟨∀ 𝑥 : 𝜑, 𝑡 ⟩ and ⟨∃ 𝑥 : 𝜑, 𝑡 ⟩

are schedule elements. 

• If 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is an atom over Σ such that 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ) for some 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }, and 𝐹 Π a tuple of induction hypotheses over Σ for the predicates in Π = def (Φ𝑖 ), then ⟨𝑃 (¯𝑡 ), 𝐹 Π⟩ is a schedule element. A schedule (𝑒 𝑖 )𝑖 is a sequence of schedule elements in which every schedule element appears infinitely often. Henceforth, we fix a schedule (𝑒 𝑖 )𝑖 , whose existence follows from the fact that there are countably many schedule elements. For each 𝑖 ∈ N, we construct finite sets Γ𝑖 and Δ𝑖 of FO-formulas over Σ such that: 

• Γ𝑖 ⊆ Γ𝑖 +1 and Δ𝑖 ⊆ Δ𝑖 +1;

Δ𝑖 , and we call Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 the limit sequent . This sequent is not cut-free provable. Indeed, if it were, then there would exist finite sets Γ′ ⊆ Γ ∪ { Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 } and Δ′ ⊆ Δ

such that Γ′ ⊢ Δ′ is cut-free provable. This follows by a straightforward argument by induction on the height of a cut-free proof of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 . However, since Γ′ ⊆ Γ𝑖 ∪ { Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 } and Δ′ ⊆ Δ𝑖 for some 𝑖 ∈ N, this would entail that Γ𝑖 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑖 is cut-free provable, which is a contradiction. We proceed by constructing the Henkin counter-model (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) for Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 . The domain of I𝜔 

consists of all equivalence classes [𝑡 ] of terms 𝑡 ∈ Terms (Σ) under an equivalence relation ∼ over Terms (Σ):

Definition D.2. We define a binary relation ∼ on Terms (Σ) by the rules: (i) if 𝑡 = 𝑠 ∈ Γ𝜔 , then 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 ;(ii) 𝑡 ∼ 𝑡 ;(iii) if 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 , then 𝑠 ∼ 𝑡 ;(iv) if 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 and 𝑠 ∼ 𝑢 , then 𝑡 ∼ 𝑢 ;(v) if 𝑡 1 ∼ 𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 ∼ 𝑠 𝑘 and 𝑓 is a 𝑘 -ary function symbol, then 𝑓 (𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 ) ∼ 𝑓 (𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑠 𝑘 ).By (ii)-(iv), ∼ is an equivalence relation. We denote the equivalence class of a term 𝑡 ∈ Terms (Σ) under ∼ by [𝑡 ],and the set of all equivalence classes under ∼ by Terms (Σ)/∼ . We will sometimes write (𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 ) ∼ ( 𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑠 𝑘 )

for 𝑡 1 ∼ 𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 ∼ 𝑠 𝑘 , and [( 𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 )] for ([ 𝑡 1], . . . , [𝑡 𝑘 ]) .We construct I𝜔 as the last structure I𝑛 of a finite sequence of structures I0, I1, . . . , I𝑛 . In defining this sequence, we use the operators CΦ𝑖 , for 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }, as defined in Section 5.1. Since the definitions Φ𝑖 are positive, CΦ

depends only on the first argument. Hence, by abuse of notation, we simply write CΦ𝑖 (I) instead of CΦ𝑖 (I , J ) .

Lemma D.5. Let 𝑥 be an object symbol and 𝑡 and 𝑠 terms. If 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 , then Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff 

Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑠 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable for every FO -formula 𝜑 .

Proof. Let 𝑡 and 𝑠 be terms such that 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 . Then there exists a tree-shaped derivation 𝑇 for the fact that 

𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 , according to the rules (i)-(v) of Definition D.2. We show by induction on the height of 𝑇 that, for every FO-formula 𝜑 and object symbol 𝑥 , Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑠 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable: Case :

𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 is derived by (i). Then 𝑡 = 𝑠 ∈ Γ𝜔 . By application of (=L), a cut-free proof of Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] can be extended to a cut-free proof of Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑠 /𝑥 ] and vice versa. 

Case : 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 is derived by (ii). Then 𝑠  𝑡 . Trivially, Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable. 

Case : 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 is derived by (iii) from 𝑠 ∼ 𝑡 . By induction hypothesis, Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑠 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff 

Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable. Then, of course, Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑠 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable. 

Case : 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 is derived by (iv) from 𝑡 ∼ 𝑢 and 𝑢 ∼ 𝑠 for a term 𝑢 . By induction hypothesis, Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑢 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable, and Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑢 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑠 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable. Then Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑠 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable. 

Case : 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 is derived by (v) from 𝑡 1 ∼ 𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 , ∼ 𝑠 𝑘 for terms 𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 , 𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑠 𝑘 . Then 𝑡  𝑓 (𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 ) and 

𝑡  𝑓 (𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑠 𝑘 ). By induction hypothesis, we know that for every 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑘 } and every FO-formula 𝜑 , the sequent Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑡 𝑖 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑠 𝑖 /𝑥 ] is cut-free provable. We derive that 

Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑓 (𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 )/ 𝑥 ] is cut-free provable iff Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑓 (𝑠 1, 𝑡 2 . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 )/ 𝑥 ] is cut-free provable 

...

iff Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝜑 [𝑓 (𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑠 𝑘 )/ 𝑥 ] is cut-free provable. 

□

Lemma D.6. Let 𝑃 be a defined predicate of one of the definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 . If [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑃 I𝜔 , then Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢

𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable. 

Proof. We prove by induction on 𝑖 that, for every 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 } and every 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ), if [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑃 I𝜔 , then 

Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable. For each 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ), we denote by 𝑋 𝑃 the set {[ ¯𝑡 ] | Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable }.   

> 29 Note that we do not need a separate case for object symbols, as we defined them to be 0-ary function symbols. 54 •Van den Eede and Denecker

Note that if [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑋 𝑃 for some 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ), then there exists a tuple of terms ¯𝑠 such that ¯𝑠 ∼ ¯𝑡 and Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ) is cut-free provable. By repeated application of Lemma D.5, it follows that Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable as well. We will use this fact multiple times in the remainder of the proof. Let J ≔ I𝜔 |pars (Φ𝑖 ) [def (Φ𝑖 ) : 𝑋 def (Φ𝑖 ) ]. In words, J is the restriction of I𝜔 to the parameters of Φ𝑖 , expanded to interpret the defined predicates 𝑃 of Φ by the sets 𝑋 𝑃 . We will show that J satisfies: (i) for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ): if 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ∈ Γ𝜔 , then [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑃 J ;(ii) CΦ𝑖 (J ) ⊑ J .Since, by Definition D.3, I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) is the least such structure, this will entail that I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) ⊑ J , and hence that 

𝑃 I𝜔 = 𝑃 I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) ⊆ 𝑃 J = 𝑋 𝑃 

for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ). By definition of 𝑋 𝑃 , this means that if [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑃 I𝜔 , then Γ𝜔 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable, thereby finishing the proof. For (i), let 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ), and assume that 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ∈ Γ𝜔 . Then Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable via (ax). It thus remains to show (ii), i.e., that CΦ𝑖 (J ) ⊑ J . Let 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ). We need to show that 𝑃 CΦ𝑖 ( J ) ⊆ 𝑃 J .Take [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑃 CΦ𝑖 ( J ) . By definition of CΦ𝑖 , this means that J [ ¯𝑦 : [¯𝑡 ]] | = 𝜑 𝑃 ( ¯𝑦 ), where ¯𝑦 is an ar (𝑃 )-tuple of object symbols not occurring freely in the body 𝜑 of any definitional rule of Φ of the form ∀¯𝑥 : 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ← 𝜑 .Since ¯𝑡 J = ¯𝑡 I𝜔 = [¯𝑡 ] by Proposition D.4, this implies J |= 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ). We claim that Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable. Via (def R), this claim entails that Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable, and hence, that 

[¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑋 𝑃 = 𝑃 J , finishing the proof. We prove the claim by showing that if J | = 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ), then Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 )

is cut-free provable. We do so by structural induction on 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ):

Case : 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is of the form 𝑡 = 𝑠 . Then 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 . Via ( =R), Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑡 = 𝑡 is cut-free provable. By Lemma D.5, 

Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑡 = 𝑠 is cut-free provable as well. 

Case : 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is of the form 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ). The predicate symbol 𝑄 is either defined by Φ𝑖 , by Φ𝑗 for some 𝑗 < 𝑖 , or not defined by any Φ𝑗 for 𝑗 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }. (Note that, since Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is a canonical sequent, 𝑄 cannot be defined by Φ𝑗 for any 𝑗 > 𝑖 .) 

Subcase : 𝑄 is defined by Φ𝑖 . Since J | = 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ), we have that [¯𝑠 ] ∈ 𝑋 𝑄 , which implies that Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑄 (¯𝑠 )

by the above observation. 

Subcase : 𝑄 is defined by Φ𝑗 for some 𝑗 < 𝑖 . Since J and I𝜔 coincide on 𝑄 , we have that [¯𝑠 ] ∈ 𝑄 I𝜔 . By (the first) induction hypothesis, Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) is cut-free provable. 

Subcase : 𝑄 is not defined by any Φ𝑗 for 𝑗 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }. Since J and I𝜔 coincide on 𝑄 , we have that [¯𝑠 ] ∈ 𝑄 I𝜔 .By definition of I𝜔 , there exists a tuple of terms ¯𝑢 such that ¯𝑢 ∼ ¯𝑠 and 𝑄 ( ¯𝑢 ) ∈ Γ𝜔 . With a single application of (ax), 

Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑄 ( ¯𝑢 ) is cut-free provable By repeated application of Lemma D.5, it follows that Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢

𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) is cut-free provable as well. 

Case : 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is of the form 𝜑 1 ∧ 𝜑 2. Then J |= 𝜑 1 and J |= 𝜑 2. By (the second) induction hypothe-sis, Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝜑 1 and Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝜑 2 are cut-free provable, and through application of ( ∧R), so is 

Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝜑 1 ∧ 𝜑 2.

Case : 𝜑 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is of the form 𝜑 1 ∨ 𝜑 2. Then J |= 𝜑 1 or J |= 𝜑 2. By (the second) induction hypothesis, 

Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝜑 1 or Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝜑 2 is cut-free provable, and through application of (wk) and ( ∨R), so is Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝜑 1 ∨ 𝜑 2. □

Lemma D.7. I𝜔 |= 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Γ𝜔 and I𝜔 ̸ |= 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Δ𝜔 .

Proof. We prove by structural induction on 𝜑 that if 𝜑 ∈ Γ𝜔 , then I𝜔 |= 𝜑 , and if 𝜑 ∈ Δ𝜔 , then I𝜔 ̸ |= 𝜑 for all FO-formulas 𝜑 .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form 𝑡 = 𝑠 . If 𝑡 = 𝑠 ∈ Γ𝜔 , then 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 by rule (i) of Definition D.2. Consequently, 𝑡 I𝜔 = [𝑡 ] =

[𝑠 ] = 𝑠 I𝜔 , and hence I𝜔 |= 𝑡 = 𝑠 .A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions • 55 

Now assume that 𝜑 ∈ Δ𝜔 and suppose for contradiction that I𝜔 |= 𝑡 = 𝑠 . Then [𝑡 ] = 𝑡 I𝜔 = 𝑠 I𝜔 = [𝑠 ], whence 

𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 . Since Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑡 = 𝑡 is obviously cut-free provable, so is Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑡 = 𝑠 , by Lemma D.5. However, this implies that Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 is cut-free provable, which is a contradiction. Thus, I𝜔 ̸ |= 𝑡 = 𝑠 .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ). If 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ∈ Γ𝜔 , then [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑃 I𝜔 by definition of I𝜔 , whence I𝜔 |= 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ).Now assume that 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ∈ Δ𝜔 , and suppose for contradiction that I𝜔 |= 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ). Then [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑃 I𝜔 . Consequently, 

Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable. Indeed, if 𝑃 is defined by one of the definitions Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 , this follows from Lemma D.6. Otherwise, there exists a tuple of terms ¯𝑠 such that ¯𝑠 ∼ ¯𝑡 such that 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ) ∈ Γ𝜔 , by definition of I𝜔 . Through (ax), Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑠 ) is cut-free provable, and by repeated application of Lemma D.5, so is 

Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ). Thus, in either case, Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is cut-free provable. However, since 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ∈ Δ𝜔 ,we obtain that Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 is cut-free provable, which is a contradiction. 

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ¬𝜓 . If ¬𝜓 ∈ Γ𝜔 , then 𝜓 ∈ Δ𝜔 by construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 . Indeed, suppose that ¬𝜓 ∈ Γ𝜔 . Then ¬𝜓 ∈ Γ𝑗 for some 𝑗 ∈ N. Since ¬𝜓 occurs infinitely often on the schedule (𝑒 𝑖 )𝑖 , it occurs as the 𝑖 -th schedule element 𝑒 𝑖 for some 𝑖 > 𝑗 . By construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 , the set Δ𝑗 +1 contains 𝜓 , and hence, so does Δ𝜔 . By induction hypothesis, I𝜔 ̸ |= 𝜓 . Consequently, I𝜔 |= ¬𝜓 .If ¬𝜓 ∈ Δ𝜔 , then 𝜓 ∈ Γ𝜔 by a similar reasoning as before. By induction hypothesis, I𝜔 |= 𝜓 . Hence, I𝜔 ̸ |= ¬𝜓 .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form 𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 . If 𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 ∈ Γ𝜔 , then 𝜓, 𝜒 ∈ Γ𝜔 by construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 . By induction hypothesis, I𝜔 |= 𝜓 and I𝜔 |= 𝜒 . Hence, I𝜔 |= 𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 .If 𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 ∈ Δ𝜔 , then 𝜓 ∈ Δ𝜔 or 𝜒 ∈ Δ𝜔 by construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 . By induction hypothesis, I𝜔 ̸ |= 𝜓 

or I𝜔 ̸ |= 𝜒 . Hence, I𝜔 ̸ |= 𝜓 ∧ 𝜒 .

Cases : 𝜑 is of the form 𝜓 ∨ 𝜒 , 𝜓 ⇒ 𝜒 or 𝜓 ⇔ 𝜒 . These cases are similar to the previous case. 

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 . If ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 ∈ Γ𝜔 , then 𝜓 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] ∈ Γ𝜔 for any term 𝑡 , by construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢

Δ𝜔 . Indeed, suppose that ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 ∈ Γ𝜔 . Then ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 ∈ Γ𝑗 for some 𝑗 ∈ N. Let 𝑡 be a term. Since ⟨∀ 𝑥 : 𝜓, 𝑡 ⟩ occurs infinitely often on the schedule (𝑒 𝑖 )𝑖 , it occurs as the 𝑖 -th schedule element of (𝑒 𝑖 )𝑖 for some 𝑖 > 𝑗 . By construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 , the set Γ𝑖 +1 contains 𝜓 [𝑡 /𝑥 ]. Hence, 𝜓 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] ∈ Γ𝜔 . By induction hypothesis, I𝜔 |= 𝜓 [𝑡 /𝑥 ].Since 𝑡 I𝜔 = [𝑡 ] by Proposition D.4, this implies I𝜔 [𝑥 : [𝑡 ]]̸ | = 𝜓 . Since any domain element of I𝜔 is of the form 

[𝑡 ] for some term 𝑡 , this shows that I𝜔 |= ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 .If ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 ∈ Δ𝜔 , then, by construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 , the set Δ𝜔 contains 𝜓 [𝑧 /𝑥 ] for some object symbol 

𝑧 . By induction hypothesis, I𝜔 ̸ |= 𝜓 [𝑧 /𝑥 ]. Since 𝑧 I𝜔 = [𝑧 ] by definition of I𝜔 , this implies I𝜔 [𝑥 : [𝑧 ]]̸ | = 𝜓 .Consequently, I𝜔 ̸ |= ∀𝑥 : 𝜓 .

Case : 𝜑 is of the form ∃𝑥 : 𝜓 . This case is similar to the previous case. □

for all 𝑘 ∈ N.

Lemma D.10. (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) | =H Φ𝑖 for all 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }.56 • Van den Eede and Denecker 

Proof. Take 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 }. We need to show that I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) is the least pre-fixed H𝜔 -point of CΦ𝑖 expanding 

I𝜔 |pars (Φ𝑖 ) . Let H𝜔 = {𝐻 𝑘 | 𝑘 ∈ N}. First, I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) is an H𝜔 -point. This is because for every 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ),we can write 𝑃 I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) as {[ ¯𝑡 ] ∈ dom (I 𝜔 ) | I 𝜔 |= 𝑃 (¯𝑡 )} , and since 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) is an FO-formula, 𝑃 I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) ∈ 𝐻 ar (𝑃 ) .Furthermore, I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) is a pre-fixpoint of CΦ𝑖 by definition of I𝜔 . It thus remains to show that I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) is the 

least pre-fixed H𝜔 -point of CΦ𝑖 expanding I𝜔 |pars (Φ𝑖 ) . Let J be a pre-fixed H𝜔 -point of CΦ𝑖 expanding I𝜔 |pars (Φ𝑖 ) .We will show that: (i) [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑃 J if 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ∈ Γ𝜔 for all 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 );(ii) CΦ𝑖 (J ) ⊑ J .Since, by definition, I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) is the least sym (Φ𝑖 )-structure expanding I𝜔 |pars (Φ𝑖 ) that satisfies these properties, this implies that I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) ⊑ J , showing that I𝜔 |sym (Φ𝑖 ) is indeed the least pre-fixed H𝜔 -point of CΦ𝑖 expanding 

I𝜔 |pars (Φ𝑖 ) . Note that (ii) is immediately satisfied, as J is a pre-fixed H𝜔 -point of CΦ𝑖 . It thus remains to show (i). Since J is an H𝜔 -point, there exists, for every 𝑃 ∈ def (Φ𝑖 ), an FO-formula 𝐹 𝑃 and an ar (𝑃 )-tuple ¯𝑧 𝑃 of distinct object symbols such that 

𝑃 J = {[ ¯𝑡 ] ∈ dom (J ) ar (𝑃 ) | I 𝜔 |= 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑡 /¯𝑧 𝑃 ]} . (25) Suppose that 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ∈ Γ𝜔 . Then 𝑃 (¯𝑡 ) ∈ Γ𝑗 for some 𝑗 ∈ N. Consider the schedule element ⟨𝑃 (¯𝑡 ), 𝐹 Π⟩, where 

Π = def (Φ𝑖 ). It occurs infinitely often in the schedule (𝑒 𝑖 )𝑖 . By construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝜔 , the sequent 

Γ𝑗 +1, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑗 +1 is one of the premises of the derivation minor premises Γ𝑖 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 , 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑡 ] ⊢ Δ𝑖 (def L) 

Γ𝑖 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑖 

where the minor premises Γ𝑖 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ,𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ𝑖 correspond to the definitional rules ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 

in Φ𝑖 . We claim that Γ𝑗 +1, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑗 +1 is the major premise Γ𝑗 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 , 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑡 ] ⊢ Δ𝑗 . This would mean that 

𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑡 ] ∈ Γ𝜔 , which would imply that I𝜔 |= 𝐹 𝑃 [¯𝑡 ] by Lemma D.7, and that [¯𝑡 ] ∈ 𝑃 J by (25), thus proving (i). We show the claim by contradiction. Suppose that Γ𝑗 +1, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ𝑗 +1 were one of the minor premises. Then it would be of the form Γ𝑗 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ⊢ 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], Δ𝑗 for a definitional rule ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑠 ) ← 𝜓 in Φ𝑖 . Then 

𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ∈ Γ𝜔 and 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ] ∈ Δ𝜔 . By Lemma D.7, the latter entails that I𝜔 ̸ |= 𝐹 𝑄 [¯𝑠 ], and by (25), [¯𝑠 ] ∉ 𝑄 J . We now show that J | = 𝜓 . It now suffices to show that J | = 𝜓 . Indeed, by (ii), J | = 𝜓 implies that [¯𝑠 ] ∈ 𝑄 J , yielding the desired contradiction. We prove that J | = 𝜓 by structural induction on 𝜓 , using the fact that 𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ∈ Γ𝜔 .

Case : 𝜓 is of the form 𝑡 = 𝑠 . Then (𝑡 = 𝑠 )  𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ∈ Γ𝜔 . By rule (i) of Definition D.2, this means that 𝑡 ∼ 𝑠 .Thus, J | = 𝑡 = 𝑠 .

Case : 𝜓 is of the form 𝑅 ( ¯𝑣 ).

Subcase : 𝑅 is defined by Φ𝑖 . Then 𝐹 𝑅 [¯𝑣 ]  𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ∈ Γ𝜔 . By Lemma D.7, I𝜔 |= 𝐹 𝑅 [¯𝑣 ]. By (25), [¯𝑣 ] ∈ 𝑅 J .Therefore, J | = 𝑅 ( ¯𝑣 ).

Subcase : 𝑅 is not defined by Φ𝑖 . Then 𝑅 ( ¯𝑣 )  𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ∈ Γ𝜔 . By Lemma D.7, I𝜔 |= 𝑅 ( ¯𝑣 ), and since J and I𝜔 

differ only on def (Φ𝑖 ), J | = 𝑅 ( ¯𝑣 ).

Case : 𝜓 is of the form 𝜓 1 ∧𝜓 2. Then 𝜓 1 [𝐹 Π/Π] ∧ 𝜓 2 [𝐹 Π/Π]  𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ∈ Γ𝜔 . By construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢

Δ𝜔 , the set Γ𝜔 contains 𝜓 1 [𝐹 Π/Π] and 𝜓 2 [𝐹 Π/Π]. By (the last) induction hypothesis, J | = 𝜓 1 and J | = 𝜓 1. Hence, 

J | = 𝜓 1 ∧ 𝜓 2.

Case : 𝜓 is of the form 𝜓 1 ∨𝜓 2. Then 𝜓 1 [𝐹 Π/Π] ∨ 𝜓 2 [𝐹 Π/Π]  𝜓 [𝐹 Π/Π] ∈ Γ𝜔 . By construction of Γ𝜔 , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢

Δ𝜔 the set Γ𝜔 contains 𝜓 1 [𝐹 Π/Π] or 𝜓 2 [𝐹 Π/Π]. By (the last) induction hypothesis, J | = 𝜓 1 or J | = 𝜓 1. Hence, 

J | = 𝜓 1 ∨ 𝜓 2. □

In conclusion, we have constructed a Henkin structure (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) (Lemma D.9) such that (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) | =H Φ𝑖 for all 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 } (Lemma D.10), (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) | =H 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Γ𝜔 and (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 )̸ | =H 𝜑 for all 𝜑 ∈ Δ𝜔 (Lemma D.7). A Sequent Calculus for General Inductive Definitions • 57 

Since Γ ⊆ Γ𝜔 and Δ ⊆ Δ𝜔 , (I 𝜔 , H𝜔 ) is a counter-model of the original sequent Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ. This finishes the proof of Theorem 8.5.

Lemma D.12. Let Γ and Δ be sets of FO(ID) -formulas and 𝜑 and FO(ID) -formula. If Γ ⊢ ¬ 𝜑, Δ is cut-free provable, then so is Γ, 𝜑 ⊢ Δ.

Γ ⊢ ¬ 𝜑, Δ

Then the subtree of 𝑇 with root labeled by Γ, 𝜑 ⊢ Δ is a cut-free proof of Γ, 𝜑 ⊢ Δ.

Γ,𝜓 ⊢ ¬ 𝜑, Δ′

(¬R)

where Δ′ ≔ Δ \ {¬ 𝜓 }. By induction hypothesis, Γ,𝜓, 𝜑 ⊢ Δ′ is cut-free provable. By application of ( ¬R), so is 

Γ, 𝜑 ⊢ ¬ 𝜓, Δ′. □

Lemma D.13. Let Γ and Δ be sets of FO(ID) -formulas. Let 𝑄 be a 𝑘 -ary predicate symbol, 𝑖 ∈ { 0, . . . , 𝑘 }, ¯𝑡 an 

𝑖 -tuple of terms, and ¯𝑦 a (𝑘 − 𝑖 )-tuple of object symbols. If Γ, ∀¯𝑦 : ¬𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) ⇔ ¬ 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) ⊢ Δ is cut-free provable, then so is Γ, ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) ∨ ¬ 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) ⊢ Δ.31

⊢ ∀ ¯𝑦 : ¬𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) ⇔ ¬ 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) is cut-free provable. Hence, by application of (wk), so is Γ, ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) ∨ ¬ 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) ⊢ Δ

in the latter case.

where Γ′ ≔ Γ \ {∀ 𝑥 : 𝜑 }. By induction hypothesis, Γ′, ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) ∨ ¬ 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ), 𝜑 [𝑡 /𝑥 ] ⊢ Δ is cut-free provable, and by application of ( ∀L), so is Γ′, ∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ) ∨ ¬ 𝑄 (¯𝑡, ¯𝑦 ), ∀𝑥 : 𝜑 ⊢ Δ. □

We are now ready to prove Lemma 8.8: 

Proof of Lemma 8.8. Suppose that Γ, Ω, Φc1, . . . , Φc 

> 𝑛

⊢ Δ is cut-free provable. Then, by repeated application of Lemma D.11 (with 𝑅 = 𝑄 c and 𝑆 = 𝑄 for each predicate symbol 𝑄 appearing negatively in the body of a rule of a definition Φ𝑖 ), so is Γ, Ω′, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ, where Ω′ consists of all formulas of the form ∀¯𝑦 : ¬𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) ⇔ ¬ 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 )

such that 𝑄 appears negatively in the body of a rule of a definition Φ𝑖 . Next, by repeated application of Lemma D.13 (with 𝑖 = 0), Γ, Ω′′ , Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is cut-free provable, where Ω′′ consists of all formulas of the form 

∀¯𝑦 : 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) ∨ ¬ 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) such that 𝑄 appears negatively in the body of a rule of a definition Φ𝑖 . It is straightforward to check that ⊢ ∀ ¯𝑦 : 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) ∨ ¬ 𝑄 ( ¯𝑦 ) is cut-free provable for every such formula. By repeated application of (wk) and (cut) on these formulas, we deduce that Γ, Φ1, . . . , Φ𝑛 ⊢ Δ is provable with elementary cuts. □
